Executor’s Liability for a Reclaimed Bequeathed Slave
Explaining the ruling that the executor is liable for the price if the bequeathed slave is reclaimed after the price is lost

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the executor’s liability when a bequeathed slave is reclaimed after the price is lost, and explain the legal rationale that the executor is the contracting party.

Core Idea
The executor bears personal liability for the price because he is the direct seller and warranty falls on him.

In this passage from Bahr al-Ra’iq, the author explains a specific scenario from Kanz al-Daqa’iq: a testator bequeaths that his slave be sold and the price given in charity; the executor sells the slave and receives the price, but the price is lost in his hand, and then the slave is reclaimed by a rightful owner. The ruling is that the executor must compensate the buyer personally. We will trace the reasoning and distinguish this from other contexts like the authorized slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Read the passage and note the key elements: the testator's bequest, the executor's sale, the loss of the price, and the reclamation of the slave.
The scenario is in the matn quoted: 'if the executor sold a slave that was bequeathed to be sold and to give charity with its price, and the slave is reclaimed after the price has perished with him'.
2
State the Ruling
The ruling is that the executor is liable (ḍamān) to the buyer for the price. Write the exact wording from the text.
Text: 'فإن استحق العبد بعد هلاك ثمنه ضمن الوصي الثمن للمشتري'
3
Identify the Legal Rationale
The passage gives the reason: the executor is the contracting party (العاقد), so the warranty (العهدة) is upon him. The buyer gave the price in exchange for the slave, but the slave did not become his; thus the executor took the buyer's money without consent and must return it.
This is a talil reasoning: the executor's direct involvement as seller creates liability.
4
Distinguish from the Authorized Slave Case
The text contrasts with the case of a slave authorized to trade (مأذون له في التجارة). There, the master cannot sell the slave because creditors have a claim on the slave's earnings. Here, no such creditors exist, so the executor's action is not similarly restricted.
This distinction highlights that the executor's liability is based on his role as seller, not on any limitation from creditors.
5
Note the Author's Promise to Cover Other Cases
The author notes that the matn did not address other instances of executor's liability (e.g., in borrowing, deposit, etc.), and he will mention them later. This shows the importance of completing the legal picture.
This step reminds us that the passage is part of a larger discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوصايا/ باب الوصي وما يملكه والتصدق بثمنه إن استحق العبد بعد هلاك ثمنه عنده ويرجع في تركة الميت وفي مال بخلاف العبد المأذون له في التجارة حيث لا يجوز للمولى بيعة لأن الغرماء لهم حق الاستيفاء بخلاف ما نحن فيه . قال رحمه الله : ( وضمن الوصي أن باع عبداً أوصى ببيعه والتصدق بثمنه إن استحق العبد بعد هلاك ثمنه عنده معناه إذا أوصى ببيع عبده والتصدق بثمنه على المساكين فباع الوصي العبد وقبض الثمن فضاع الثمن في يده وهو المراد بالهلاك المذكور في المختصر، ثم استحق العبد بعد ذلك ضمن الوصي الثمن للمشتري لأنه هو العاقد فتكون العهدة عليه لأن المشتري منه لم يرض ببدل الثمن إلا ليسلم له المبيع ولم يسلم فقد أخذ البائع وهو الوصي مال الغير بغير رضاه فيجب عليه رده. ولم يتعرض لضمان الوصي في الاستقراض ولا في الطعام والوديعة والبيع بطلب الغرماء أو بغير طلب ونحن نذكر ذلك تتميماً للفائدة .
Book of Wills / Chapter of the Executor and What He Possesses: and the charity with its price if the slave is taken back after his price has perished in his hand, and he recovers from the deceased's estate and from the property of the child, contrary to the authorized slave in trade where it is not permissible for the master to sell him because the creditors have the right of collection, unlike here. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the executor is liable if he sold a slave that was bequeathed to be sold and to give charity with its price; if the slave is reclaimed after the price has perished with him, the meaning is: if he bequeaths to sell his slave and give charity with its price to the poor, and the executor sold the slave and received the price, and the price was lost in his hand—that is meant by perishing mentioned in al-Mukhtasar—then the slave was reclaimed thereafter, the executor is liable for the price to the buyer because he is the contracting party, so the warranty is upon him because the buyer from him did not consent to the exchange of the price except that the sold item be delivered to him, and it was not delivered; thus the seller, who is the executor, has taken the property of another without his consent, so it is obligatory for him to return it. And he did not address the executor's liability in borrowing, nor in food, deposit, and sale at the request of creditors or without request, and we mention that to complete the benefit.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by quoting the matn: the ruling that if the executor sells a slave that the testator bequeathed to be sold and the price given in charity, and after the executor has taken the price and it is lost, the slave is reclaimed (i.e., someone proves ownership and takes it back), then the executor is liable to the buyer for the price. The reason given is that the executor is the direct seller (العاقد), so the warranty (العهدة) falls on him. The buyer gave the price in exchange for the slave, but since the slave did not become his, the executor has taken the buyer's money without consent, so he must return it. The text distinguishes this from the case of an authorized slave (مأذون له في التجارة) where the master cannot sell him because creditors have a claim on the slave's earnings. Here, the executor is not constrained by such creditors. The author then notes that the matn did not cover other cases of executor's liability (like in borrowing, feeding, deposit, or sale to creditors), and promises to mention them later for completeness.

Key Terms
وصي (wasī): The person appointed by the testator to execute his will or manage the estate; the executor.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; obligation to compensate for loss or damage, here specifically the executor's liability to pay the price to the buyer.
عهدة (ʿuhda): Warranty or responsibility attached to the seller in a sale; the obligation to deliver sound title.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the executor liable to the buyer when the bequeathed slave is reclaimed after the price is lost?
A) Because he acted without the testator's permission
B) Because he is the contracting party (العاقد) and the warranty (العهدة) is upon him
C) Because the creditors have a right to the price
D) Because the slave was authorized for trade

Key Takeaway
The executor is personally liable for the price when a bequeathed slave is reclaimed after the price is lost, because as the direct seller, he bears the warranty of title.

Cases of Executor's Liability: Borrowing, Testimony, and Feeding Bequests
Detailing various scenarios where the executor is or is not liable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an executor is liable and when he is not in transactions involving orphan property, testimony, and feeding bequests, citing the Hanafi rulings from al-Mabsut.

Core Idea
An executor is liable only when he acts beyond his authority or causes harm; feeding by will order does not incur liability.

This passage from al-Mabsut, a foundational Hanafi text, clarifies executor liability in three distinct scenarios: lending orphan money, testifying after paying a debt, and executing a feeding bequest. Understanding these nuances is essential for anyone managing estates or issuing fatwas on guardianship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each case, specify what action the executor took and whether the text declares him liable (ḍamān) or not.
2
Determine the executor's authority
Check whether the executor had the legal authority to perform the act. If he lacked authority (e.g., cannot lend orphan's money), liability may shift to the actual doer.
3
Analyze the consequence of the act
See if the act caused harm or benefit. For example, in the testimony case, paying first then testifying benefits the executors personally, so testimony is rejected and they are liable.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
In the feeding case, Muhammad holds no liability; Abu Yusuf distinguishes qiyas (liable) from istihsan (not liable). Understand the basis of each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال . المبسوط : فالوصي تارة يضمن وتارة لا يضمن فإذا أمر الوصي المستودع أن يقرض مال اليتيم من جهته فلا يصير فأقرض ضمن المستودع لأن الوصي لا يملك الإقراض من مال الصبي فلا يملك التوكيل والأمر به فلم يصح الأمر بالإقراض. ولو قضى الوصيان ديناً للرجل ثم شهدا أن له على الميت ديناً لم يجر ويضمنان إن ظهر دين آخر لأنهما بشهادتهما يدفعان عن أنفسهما مغرماً لأنهما صارا ضامنين ما دفعا إلى الأول لأنهما دفعا بغير أمر القاضي، ولو شهدا به قبل أن يقضيا جاز لأنهما بشهادتهما لم يجز إلى أنفسهما نفعاً ولا يدفعان مغرماً وهو لزوم قضاء الدين. ومسائل الإطعام على فصول: الأول لو أوصى بأن يطعم عشرة مساكين لكفارة يمينه وغدى الوصي عشرة ثم ماتوا قال محمد رحمه الله : يغدي ويعشي عشرة أخرى ولا يضمن لأنه غداهم بأمر الموصي لأن التغدية اطعام ولكنه لم يكمل وفات الإكمال لا بمعنى متعدياً. وإن قال اطعموا عني عشرة مساكين غداء وعشاء ولم يسم فغدى عشرة ثم ماتوا فإنه يعشي عشرة سواهم لأن الواجب في كفارة اليمين سد عشرة خلات ورد عشرة جوعات، وذلك يحصل بالتغدية والتعشية. وبالموت فات ذلك فيغدي ويعشي غيرهم. فأما إذا نص على الإطعام غداء وعشاء فالجمع والتفريق سواء. وروى هشام عن أبي يوسف أنه إن قال أطعم عني عشرة مساكين فغدى عشرة ثم ماتوا يضمن الوصي قياساً ولا يضمن استحساناً ويعشي غيرهم لأنه أمرهم بالإطعام مطلقاً فالتحق بالإطعام الواجب شرعاً في الكفارة لأنه نص على الغداء والعشاء فسواء فرق أو جمع جاز.
Al-Mabsut says: The executor is sometimes liable and sometimes not liable. If the executor orders the depositary to lend the orphan's money from his side, and he lends, the depositary is liable because the executor does not have the authority to lend from the orphan's property, so he cannot authorize it, thus the order to lend is invalid. And if two executors pay a debt to a man, then testify that he has a debt against the deceased, their testimony is not accepted, and they are liable if another debt appears, because by their testimony they ward off a burden from themselves, since they became guarantors for what they paid to the first, because they paid without the judge's order. And if they testified before paying, it is permissible, because they did not gain a benefit by their testimony nor do they ward off a burden, which is the obligation to pay the debt. And the issues of feeding are in sections: First, if he bequeaths to feed ten poor people for his expiation oath, and the executor lunches ten who then die, Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: He should give lunch and dinner to another ten, and he is not liable because he lunched them by the testator's order, because lunch is feeding, but he did not complete it, and the failure to complete is not a transgression. And if he said 'Feed for me ten poor people lunch and dinner' without specifying, and he lunched ten who then died, then he should dinner another ten, because what is obligatory in expiation oath is to satiate ten stomachs and to satisfy ten hungers, which is achieved by lunch and dinner. By their death that is lost, so he lunches and dinners others. But if he specified feeding lunch and dinner, then separate or combined is the same. Hisham narrated from Abu Yusuf that if he said 'Feed for me ten poor people' and he lunched ten who then died, the executor is liable by analogy and not liable by istihsan, and he dinners others, because he ordered them to feed absolutely, so it is linked to the feeding that is obligatory by law in expiation, because he specified lunch and dinner, so whether separated or combined it is permissible.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is from al-Mabsut, detailing when the executor is liable. The first case: if the executor instructs a depositary to lend the orphan's money, and the depositary does so, the depositary is liable because the executor lacks the authority to lend the orphan's property, so his instruction is void. The second case: two executors pay a debt to a man, then testify that the same man had a debt on the deceased. Their testimony is rejected because they are protecting themselves from personal liability. If they had testified before paying, it would be accepted. The third section deals with feeding bequests. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) rules that if the executor feeds ten poor people for an expiation oath and they die before dinner, he must feed another ten without liability because he acted per the will. If the testator said 'feed lunch and dinner' without specifying, and the fed die, he must dinner others. But if the testator specified that feeding includes both lunch and dinner, then feeding separately or combined is sufficient. A narration from Abu Yusuf says if the testator said 'feed' absolutely, then by analogy the executor is liable if the fed die before dinner, but by istihsan he is not liable and must feed others.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; here specifically the executor's legal responsibility to compensate.
استقراض (istiqrāḍ): A loan taken by the executor from the orphan's property, which he is not authorized to do.
كفارة يمين (kaffārat yamīn): The expiation for a broken oath, which is feeding or clothing ten poor persons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a testator says 'Feed ten poor people for my expiation oath' without specifying lunch and dinner, and the executor lunches ten who then die, what must the executor do?
A) He is not liable and need not feed anymore.
B) He is liable and must compensate the estate.
C) He must give dinner to ten others and is not liable.
D) He must give dinner to the same ten (now dead) is not possible, so nothing.

Key Takeaway
Executor liability depends on whether the act is within his authority and whether it causes personal benefit or harm; feeding per the testator's order does not create liability even if the beneficiaries die before completion.

Executor's Liability in Deposit, Sale, and Freeing a Slave
Rulings on executor's liability in deposit, debtor's sale, and freeing a slave who committed a crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an executor or trustee is personally liable for loss in three scenarios: misdelivery of a deposit, sale on behalf of creditors, and freeing a slave who has committed a crime.

Core Idea
An executor or trustee is liable when he acts without authority or fails to guarantee existing obligations.

This lesson examines several rulings from the Hanafi school regarding the liability of an executor (waṣī) or trustee (amīn). The passage covers cases involving misdelivery of a deposit, mixing orphan property, sale of estate assets for creditors, and freeing a slave after a crime. Understanding when liability attaches helps the executor avoid personal loss and fulfill his duties correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role and authority
Determine whether the person is an executor, trustee, or agent. Check the scope of their authority: are they acting under a will, by court order, or by private agreement?
2
Analyse the action and its context
Examine the specific action: delivering a deposit, selling slaves, freeing a slave. Note any existing claims: other heirs, debts, or crimes.
3
Check for knowledge and authorization
Did the actor know about the claim? Was the action authorized by all parties with rights? Unauthorized action triggers liability; authorized action shifts liability to the principal.
4
Apply the liability rule
If the actor exceeded authority or failed to guarantee a known claim, he is personally liable. If he acted within proper authorization, liability may be transferred to the heirs or creditors.
5
Note intra-Hanafi variations
Be aware of differences: Abu Yusuf exempts executor mixing property; al-Saffar and Abu al-Layth differ on relatives eating from a sick person's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل أودع رجلاً مالاً وقال إن مت فادفعه إلى ابني فدفعه إليه وله وارث غيره ضمن حصته ولا يكون هذا وصياً لأنه لم يفوض إليه التصرف في التركة فبقي أميناً للورثة، والأمين إذا دفع مال الورثة إلى أحدهم ضمن وإن قال ادفعه إلى فلان غير وارث ضمن المال الذي دفعه إليه . قال أبو يوسف : إذا خلط الوصي مال اليتيم بماله فضاع فلا ضمان عليه لأن له ولاية حفظه كيفما كان مريض اجتمع عنده قرابته يأكلون من ماله قال أبو القاسم الصفار : إن من أكلوا بأمر المريض فمن كان منهم وارثاً ،ضمن ومن كان غير وارث حسب ذلك ثلثه . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : احتاج المريض إلى تعاهدهم في مرضه فأكلوا معه ومع عياله بغير إسراف فلا ضمان عليهم رجل مات وعليه دين فباع وصيه رقيقه للغرماء وقبض الثمن فضاع عنده أو مات بعض الرقيق في يد الوصي قبل أن يسلم إلى المشتري فالمشتري يرجع بالثمن على الوصي ويرجع به الوصي على الغرماء لأنه في البيع عامل للغرماء، ومن عمل لغيره ولحقه فيه ضمان رجع به على المعمول له ولو استحق العبد ورجع المشتري بالثمن على لم يرجع الوصي بالثمن على الغرماء إلا أن يكون الغرماء أمروه ببيعه، وكذلك لو قال الوصي رجع الغرماء له بع رقيق الميت واقضنا لم يرجع عليهم ولو كانوا قالوا بع عبد فلان هذا بالثمن عليهم لأنهم عينوه إلا أن يكون الثمن من دينهم فلا يرجع عليهم بأكثر من دينهم . ولو قال له بع هذا العبد فإنه لفلان فقال الوصي لا أبيعه ثم باعه ثم استحق وقد ضاع الثمن رجع به الوصي على الغريم ولو لم يكن على الميت دين ولكن الوصي باع الرقيق للورثة الكبار فهم في جميع هذا الوجوه كلها بمنزلة الغرماء، وإن كانوا صغاراً لم يرجع عليهم في الاستحقاق. ولو باع القاضي رقيق الميت للغرماء فضاع الثمن عنده ثم استحق الرقيق رجع المشتري بالثمن على الغرماء لا على القاضي لأنهم بمنزلة بيع الغرماء كأنهم نالوا البيع بأنفسهم. رجل أوصى بعتق عبد ثم جنى العبد جناية بعد موت الموصى فأعتقه الوصي وهو يعلم بالجناية فهو ضامن أرش الجناية، وإن لم يعلم ضمن قيمته ولا بذلك على أحد يرجع لأن الميت إنما أوصى بعتقه قبل أن يجني فلما جنى لم يكن للوصي أن يعتقه إلا أن يضمن الجناية عنه، فإذا أعتقه فهو متطوع في عتقه والجناية لازمة له؛ فإن قال الوصي عند القاضي قد اخترت إمساك العبد وأشهد على نفسه بذلك شهوداً فليس له أن يرجع ويدفع العبد، فإن لم يكن لهم مال غير العبد فعليه أن يبيع ويؤدي أرش الجناية من ثمنه، فإن مات العبد قبل أن يبيعه بعد ما اختاره فالجناية دين على الإيتام حتى يؤدونها .
A man deposited money with a man and said: If I die, give it to my son. He gave it to him while he had another heir, so he is liable for his share, and this is not an executor because he was not delegated to manage the estate, so he remains a trustee for the heirs; and when a trustee pays the heirs' property to one of them, he is liable. And if he said 'Give it to so-and-so who is not an heir,' he is liable for the money he gave to him. Abu Yusuf said: If the executor mixes the orphan's property with his own and it is lost, there is no liability on him because he has the authority to keep it however he wishes. A sick person; his relatives gathered around him eating from his property. Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar said: If they ate by the patient's order, then whoever is an heir, he is liable, and whoever is not an heir, that counts from his third. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: The patient needed to take care of them in his illness, so they ate with him and his family without extravagance, so there is no liability on them. A man died with a debt, and his executor sold his slaves to the creditors and took the price which was lost in his hand, or some slaves died in the executor's hand before delivery to the buyer; then the buyer recovers the price from the executor, and the executor recovers it from the creditors because he is acting for them in the sale. Whoever acts for another and incurs liability in that action recovers from the person for whom he acted. But if the slave is reclaimed and the buyer recovers the price from him, the executor does not recover the price from the creditors unless they ordered him to sell it. Similarly, if the executor said (and the creditors said to him: Sell the dead man's slaves and pay us), he does not recover from them. And if they said: Sell this slave of such-and-such with the price on them, then he recovers from them because they designated him, unless the price is from their debt, then he does not recover more than their debt. And if someone said to him: Sell this slave, for it belongs to so-and-so, and the executor said: I will not sell, then he sold it, then it was reclaimed while the price had been lost, the executor recovers from that person. And if the dead man had no debt, but the executor sold the slaves to the adult heirs, they are in all these aspects like creditors. And if they were minors, he does not recover from them in the case of reclamation. And if the judge sold the dead man's slaves to the creditors, and the price was lost with him, then the slave was reclaimed, the buyer recovers the price from the creditors, not from the judge, because it is like the sale by the creditors themselves. A man bequeathed to free a slave, then the slave committed a crime after the testator's death. The executor freed him while knowing of the crime, so he is liable for the blood money. If he was unaware, he is liable for the value, and he does not recover from anyone because the testator only bequeathed to free him before the crime. When he committed the crime, the executor should not free him unless he guarantees the blood money. So if he frees him, he is a volunteer in freeing and the crime is binding on him. If the executor says before the judge: I have chosen to retain the slave, and he makes witnesses swear on himself, then he cannot go back and hand over the slave. If they have no property other than the slave, he must sell and pay the blood money from its price. If the slave dies before he sells after his choice, the blood money becomes a debt on the orphans until they pay it.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage covers several independent rulings: (1) A depositary who is told to give money to the testator's son after death, but there are other heirs: the depositary is liable for the share of the others because he is only a trustee, not an executor. (2) Abu Yusuf says if the executor mixes orphan's property with his own and it is lost, he is not liable because he has the authority to keep it in any permissible manner. (3) Regarding a sick person whose relatives eat from his property: if the sick person ordered them, heirs are liable (the value is deducted from their shares), non-heirs' consumption counts from the one-third (bequest). But if the sick person needed their care, there is no liability if they ate moderately. (4) A debtor dies, and the executor sells slaves to creditors. If the price is lost or slaves die before delivery, the buyer can recover the price from the executor, and the executor recovers from the creditors because he acted as their agent. However, if the creditors did not specifically authorize the sale, the executor cannot recover from them. Detailed subcases: if creditors named a specific slave, the executor recovers from them; if the executor sold to adult heirs, they are like creditors; if to minors, he cannot recover. (5) If the judge sells the slaves, the buyer recovers from creditors, not from the judge. (6) If a testator bequeaths to free a slave, and the slave commits a crime after death, the executor must not free him unless he guarantees the crime. If he frees him knowingly, he is liable for the blood money; if unaware, he is liable for the slave's value, and cannot recover from the estate. Once the executor declares in court that he retains the slave, he cannot later hand him over. If the orphans have no other property, he must sell the slave and pay the blood money from the proceeds. If the slave dies after the choice, the blood money becomes a debt on the orphans.

Key Terms
أمين (amīn): A trustee who holds property without being an executor; he is liable if he misdelivers.
غرماء (ghuramāʾ): Creditors who have a debt on the deceased; their position in the sale of the deceased's property.
جناية (jināyah): A crime, specifically here an offense by the slave that requires blood money (diya).
أرش (arsh): Blood money or compensation for injury; here specifically the compensation for the crime (جناية).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what happens if the executor mixes the orphan's property with his own and it is lost?
A) The executor is liable for the loss.
B) The executor is not liable because he has authority to keep it however he wishes.
C) The executor is liable only if he was negligent.
D) The orphan can recover from the executor's personal property.

Key Takeaway
An executor or trustee is personally liable when he acts without authority or without guaranteeing claims against the property.

Executor's Right of Recourse: From the Entire Estate as a Debt Based on Ghurur
Ruling with proof on executor's recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the executor's right of recourse after a sale is reclaimed and explain why the preferred view is recovery from the entire estate based on ghurur.

Core Idea
The executor's recourse is based on ghurur, not the will, so it is a debt payable from the entire estate.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the executor's right to recover compensation after a sale of estate property is reclaimed. The matn rules that the executor recovers from the deceased's estate. The commentary reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa initially denied recourse but later allowed full recovery, while Muhammad limited it to one-third. The author resolves the dispute by grounding the recourse in ghurur (deception), which creates a debt like any other, thereby covering the entire estate. The same principle applies when selling a minor's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The matn states that the executor recovers from the deceased's estate because he is an agent. The ruling is that the executor has a right to indemnity.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa initially held no recourse, then retracted to full estate. Muhammad held recourse from one-third. Another narration says from charity recipients.
The dispute hinges on whether the basis is agency, will, or ghurur.
3
Analyze the proofs for each view
Abu Hanifa's initial: will invalid so no agency. Muhammad: recourse by virtue of will, so limited to one-third. Author: recourse based on ghurur, which is a debt from the entire estate.
The author rejects Muhammad's analogy to the will.
4
Apply tarjih (preference)
The author prefers recourse from the entire estate because ghurur creates a debt, and debts are paid from all assets. This is the mufta bihi position.
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah.
5
Extend the ruling to the child's property
The matn applies the same rule: if the executor sells a minor's property and it is reclaimed, he recovers from the minor's property because he acted as the minor's agent.
The same agency logic applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ويرجع في تركة الميت لأنه عامل له فيرجع به في تركته كالوكيل وكان أبو حنيفة يقول أولاً: لا يرجع الوصي على أحد لأنه تبين بطلان الوصية باستحقاق العبد فلم يكن عاملاً للورثة فلا يرجع عليهم بشيء، ثم رجع إلى ما ذكره هنا ويرجع في جميع التركة. وعن محمد أنه يرجع في الثلث لأن الرجوع بحكم الوصية فيأخذ حكمها ومحل الوصية الثلث ونحن لا نسلم أنه يرجع عليه بحكم الوصية بل بحكم الغرور وذلك دين عليه والدين عليه يقضى من جميع التركة وإن كانت التركة قد هلكت أو لم يكن بها وفاء فلا يرجع بشيء كما في سائر ديون الميت وفي المنتقى : لا يرجع الوصي في مال الميت بشيء وإنما يرجع على المساكين الذين تصدق عليهم بالثمن لأنه عامل لهم فكان غرمه عليهم. قال رحمه الله : (وفي مال الطفل إن باع ماله واستحق المبيع رجع في مال الصغير) لأنه عامل له .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And he recovers from the deceased's estate because he is an agent for him, so he recovers from his estate like an agent. Abu Hanifa used to say initially: The executor does not recover from anyone, because it became clear that the will was invalid due to the reclamation of the slave, so he was not acting for the heirs, thus he does not recover from them. Then he retracted to what he mentioned here, and he recovers from the entire estate. And from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) that he recovers from one-third, because the recourse is by virtue of the will, so it takes its ruling, and the locus of the will is one-third. And we do not accept that he recovers by virtue of the will, but rather by virtue of deception (ghurur), and that is a debt upon him, and a debt is paid from the entire estate. Even if the estate has perished or there is not enough, he recovers nothing, like other debts of the deceased. And in al-Muntaqa: The executor does not recover anything from the deceased's property, but rather recovers from the poor people to whom he gave charity with the price, because he was acting for them, so his loss is on them. He (the author) said: (And in the property of the child, if he sells his property and the sold item is reclaimed, he recovers from the child's property) because he is acting for him.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This is the conclusion of the chapter, discussing the executor's right to recover (يرجع) after he has been forced to pay compensation. The matn states that he recovers from the estate of the deceased because he acted as an agent (عامل). The commentary notes that Abu Hanifa initially held that the executor cannot recover from anyone because the reclamation of the slave proves the will was invalid, so he was not an agent for the heirs. However, he later retracted this and ruled that he recovers from the entire estate. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) held that he only recovers from one-third (the maximum for bequests) because the recourse is based on the will, which is limited to one-third. The author (al-Marghinani) rejects this, arguing that the recourse is based on 'ghurur' (deception/warranty of title), which creates a debt, and debts are paid from the entire estate. If the estate has perished or is insufficient, he recovers nothing, like any other creditor. Another narration from al-Muntaqa states that the executor recovers from the poor charity recipients because he acted for them, not for the testator. Finally, the matn ends with a separate ruling: if the executor sold the property of a minor (the child) and the sale is reclaimed, he recovers from the child's property because he acted for the child. This covers the two types of property mentioned at the beginning: the deceased's estate and the child's property.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujūʿ): The executor's right to claim reimbursement from the estate or other parties after being made to pay compensation.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception or warranty of title; the idea that the executor is deceived by the apparent ownership, so the recourse is a debt like other debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, from what portion of the estate does the executor recover?
A) From one-third only, as in bequests
B) From the entire estate, as a debt
C) He does not recover at all
D) From the charity recipients only

Key Takeaway
The executor's recourse is based on ghurur (deception), making it a debt payable from the entire estate, not limited to one-third.

Definition of the Hermaphrodite and Gender Determination by Urination
Defines the hermaphrodite and provides the initial method to determine gender.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the khuntha according to the Hanafi school and state the initial method to determine gender by urination.

Core Idea
A khuntha is defined by having both male and female organs; gender is revealed by the organ of urination.

This lesson introduces the category of the hermaphrodite (khuntha) in Hanafi fiqh. We examine the definition provided by the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and the primary method to determine the gender of such a person. The passage also includes commentary that clarifies the legal scope and a subtle disagreement about whether a person with no visible organs can be considered a khuntha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Focus on the phrase 'he is one who has a female genitals (farj) and a male genitals (dhakar)'—this is the core definition of khuntha.
2
Apply the urination test
The text gives a conditional: if urine comes from the dhakar, the person is a boy; if from the farj, a girl. This is the initial ruling for gender assignment.
3
Note the dispute on definition
Al-Baqqali adds that a person with neither organ (urine exiting from the anus or elsewhere) can also be khuntha. Distinguish this from the author's primary definition.

Book of the Hermaphrodite. He is one who has a female genitals and a male genitals. If he urinates from the male genitals, then he is a boy; if he urinates from the female genitals, then he is a female; if he urinates... his speech. Book of the Hermaphrodite. One who has two organs. It is on the pattern of fu'la (with damma) from takhannuth, which is softness and brittle, hence mukhanath. It is called khuntha because it is broken and its condition is deficient compared to a man. Its plural is khunatha. In Shari'ah, what the author mentioned. In al-Nihayah: When he finished explaining rulings for one who has one organ—women and men—he began explaining rulings for one who has two organs. He presented the first because the one comes before the two, and because the first is more common and prevalent, while this is rare. I say: there is discussion. Firstly, because what is mentioned in previous books is not exclusive to one with one organ, but includes one with one organ. Do you see that the rulings passed in the book of wills, for example, apply entirely to the hermaphrodite as well, similarly in the rulings of the previous books all or most of them? So what is the meaning of his statement: 'When he finished from rulings of one with one organ, he began explaining rulings of one with two organs?' The author in al-Hidayah made two chapters: first for its definition, second for its rulings where he said: 'Chapter on its definition', then 'Chapter on its rulings'. In this book, he only began truly in explaining one who has two organs, not in explaining its rulings. He only mentioned its rulings in the second chapter after mentioning its definition in the first. If one says he also began its rulings with a ta'wil, then what is the meaning of specifying the beginning with the second in his statement: 'He began explaining rulings of one who has two organs?' And in al-'Inayah: 'When he finished from rulings of the prevalent, he mentioned rulings of the problem (al-mushkil). He said 'al-mushkil' (masculine) and did not say 'al-mushkila' (feminine) because what is not known as masculine or feminine, the default is masculine.' The author said (may Allah have mercy on him): (He is one who has a female genitals and a male genitals) meaning the hermaphrodite is one who has a woman's genitals and a man's genitals. The apparent meaning of the author's statement is that both organs are necessary. Al-Baqqali said: 'Or he may have neither male nor female genitals, and his urine exits from an opening in the anus or elsewhere.' And it is not hidden that Allah creates what He wills; He creates only male, only female, or hermaphrodite. The author said (may Allah have mercy on him): (If he urinates from the male genitals then he is a boy; if he urinates from the female genitals... (incomplete) He is rare in existence. End. Correct that.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of the Hermaphrodite (Khuntha) and provides the definition and initial ruling. The author (Ibn Nujaym) defines the khuntha as one who has both male and female genitalia. The ruling is that gender is determined by the organ from which urine exits: if from the penis, the person is a boy; if from the vagina, the person is a girl. The commentary then discusses the structure of the book: the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) placed this after discussing rulings for those with one organ. However, the commentator objects that previous rulings actually apply to khuntha as well, so why treat it separately? He notes that the author of al-Hidayah made two chapters: one for definition and one for rulings. The commentator clarifies that in this book (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq), the focus is on defining the khuntha (of two organs) before its rulings. Then al-Baqqali is cited, who includes a broader definition: a khuntha may have neither organ and instead has an opening in the anus or elsewhere. The commentator affirms that Allah creates various types. The passage ends with the beginning of the ruling on urination, but the text cuts off. The key point is that the khuntha is defined by having both organs, and the primary method to determine gender is by observing the source of urination.

Key Terms
خنثى (khuntha): A person who has both male and female genital organs (or possibly neither), and their gender is ambiguous.
فرج (farj): The female genital organ (vagina).
ذكر (dhakar): The male genital organ (penis).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Having both male and female organs.
C) Being able to urinate from two places.
D) Having no organs at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of khuntha requires both male and female genitalia, and gender is determined by the organ from which urine exits.

Secondary Sexual Characteristics for Determining Sex of a Hermaphrodite
States the secondary signs for sex determination and the prayer rule for ambiguous cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the secondary signs that determine the sex of a hermaphrodite at puberty and state the prayer ruling when signs are ambiguous.

Core Idea
A hermaphrodite's sex is determined at puberty by physical signs; ambiguity requires a special prayer placement.

In Hanafi fiqh, determining the sex of a hermaphrodite is crucial for many legal rulings. The primary method at birth is the urination test, but if inconclusive, secondary signs at puberty become decisive. This lesson explains the signs for male and female and the rule for the problematic case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Understand that this applies to a hermaphrodite (khuntha) who has reached puberty and for whom the urination test has not resolved the sex.
The urination test is the primary method; secondary signs are used only when that fails.
2
List male signs
According to the text, the signs indicating maleness at puberty are: appearance of a beard (lihyah), ability to have intercourse with women (wusul ila al-nisa'), and nocturnal emission from the male organ (ihtilam min al-dhakar).
Any one of these is sufficient to classify as male.
3
List female signs
The signs indicating femaleness are: appearance of a breast (thady), lactation (laban), and ability to be penetrated (imkan wat'ih).
Again, any one suffices.
4
Handle ambiguity
If none of these signs appear, or if conflicting signs appear (e.g., both a beard and breasts), the person is deemed problematic (mushkil).
This status triggers special rules in acts of worship.
5
Apply prayer rule
The mushkil must stand between the men's and women's rows during congregational prayer to avoid invalidating the prayer of those adjacent if classified incorrectly.
This is a precautionary measure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (إن بلغ وخرجت له لحية أو وصل إلى النساء فرجل وكذا إذا احتلم من الذكر وإن ظهر له ثدي أو لبن أو أمكن وطؤه فامرأة وإن لم تظهر له علامة أو تعارضت فمشكل) فيقف بين صف الرجال والنساء لأنه يحتمل أن يكون ذكراً ويحتمل أن يكون أنثى لأنه لو وقف في صف النساء، فإن كان ذكراً تفسد صلاته في صف النساء، ولو وقف في صف الرجال تبطل صلاته من يحاذيه إن كان أنثى فلا يتخلل الرجال ولا النساء
He (the author) said, may God have mercy on him: (If he reaches puberty and a beard appears or he can reach women, he is a man; similarly if he has a nocturnal emission from the male organ. And if a breast appears or milk or he can be penetrated, she is a woman. And if no sign appears or they conflict, he is problematic.) So he stands between the row of men and the row of women because it is possible he is a man and possible he is a woman because if he stands in the women's row, if he is a man his prayer is invalidated; if he stands in the men's row, the prayer of those adjacent is invalidated if she is a woman. So he does not enter into the rows of men nor women.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the secondary signs that determine the sex of a hermaphrodite when the earlier urination test is inconclusive. The text lists: for a male, at puberty, the appearance of a beard, ability to have sexual intercourse with women (i.e., virility), or nocturnal emission from the male organ. For a female: the appearance of breasts, lactation (milk), or being capable of being penetrated (i.e., receptive to intercourse). If none of these signs appear, or if conflicting signs appear (e.g., beard and breasts), the person remains problematic (mushkil). The text then immediately gives the rule for prayer: such a person stands between the men's and women's rows, avoiding invalidation of either. This is based on the principle of taking the precaution (ihtiyat). The passage is clear and does not include proof, but it is derived from customary biological signs recognized in fiqh.

Key Terms
لحية (lihyah): Beard, a sign of male puberty.
ثدي (thady): Breast, a sign of female puberty.
لبن (laban): Milk (lactation), a sign of female puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Appearance of a beard.
D) Lactation.

Key Takeaway
The secondary signs at puberty resolve the sex of a hermaphrodite; if ambiguous, special prayer rules apply.

Rulings for the Khuntha in Prayer, Dress, and Circumcision
Explanation of rulings for the khuntha in prayer, dress, and circumcision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the precautionary rulings for a khuntha in prayer, ihram, and circumcision, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding ihram clothing.

Core Idea
The khuntha's uncertainty requires precaution: sit like a woman, cover head, and avoid gender-specific acts.

The khuntha (hermaphrodite) presents a unique legal challenge: how to apply gender-specific rulings when the gender is unknown. Hanafi jurists resolve this through ihtiyat (precaution), choosing the option that avoids potential sin. This lesson unpacks the rulings for the khuntha in prayer, dress, and circumcision as detailed in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, including a notable disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rulings for prayer and dress
Scan the passage for specific commands: sitting like a woman, covering the head (qina'), and restrictions on jewelry and silk. Note the age distinction (pre-puberty vs. post-puberty).
The principle of ihtiyat applies even if the act is only sunnah for one gender.
2
Analyze the ihram clothing dispute
Focus on the passage where Abu Yusuf says 'la 'ilma li' (I have no knowledge) and Muhammad recommends sewn clothes for precaution. Understand each position's basis.
Muhammad's view avoids the risk of exposing 'awrah if the khuntha is female.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: what should a khuntha actually wear in ihram? Compare the two opinions and see which is preferred.
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view due to stronger precaution.
4
Examine rulings on seclusion and travel
Identify the rules for being alone with a non-mahram and traveling. Note that khuntha is treated like both genders: seclusion with a non-mahram is disliked.
The khuntha cannot travel with a non-mahram woman but can with a male mahram.
5
Understand the circumcision ruling and its rationale
Read the passage on circumcision: it is disliked for a man or woman to circumcise the khuntha. Instead, a female slave is bought. Grasp the logic of ownership and necessity.
The living khuntha can own, so buying a slave is valid; after death, ownership is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الخنثى
أنه بالغاً حراً يجب عليه ذلك . ويجلس في صلاته جلوس المرأة لأنه إن كان رجلاً فقد ترك سنة وهو جائز في الجملة، وإن كان امرأة فقد ارتكب مكروهاً بجلوسه جلوس الرجال. والأصل فيه فيما يرجع إلى العبادات قال محمد : أحب إلي أن يصلي بقناع لاحتمال أنه امرأة يريد قبل البلوغ، وإن صلى بغيره فإن كان غير بالغ لا يؤمر بالإعادة إلا استحساناً تخلقاً واعتباراً، وفي الهداية : صلى بغير قناع امرأة أن يعيد وهو الاستحسان. هذا إذا كان الخنثى مراهقاً غير بالغ، فإن كان بالغاً فإن بلغ بالسن ولم يظهر فيه شيء من علامات الرجال والنساء لا تجزيه الصلاة بغير قناع إذا كان الخنثى حراً. وفي السنغاقي وفي بعض النسخ : وإن كان بالغاً فصلى بغير قناع امرأة فإنه يعيد وهذا بطريق الاحتياط ؛ هكذا لفظ المبسوط. ولم يتعرض فيه أن طريق الاحتياط فيه على وجه الاستحباب أو على وجه الوجوب والظاهر هو الوجوب قال : ويجلس في صلاته كجلوس المرأة. ولو أحرم هذا الخنثى وقد راهق ولم يبلغ ولم يستبن امرأة قال أبو يوسف : لا علم لي .بلباسه. وقال محمد: إن لبس المخيط كان أحوط لجواز أنها أنثى فلا يحل لها كشف العورة قال : ويكره أن يلبس الحلي. وأراد به ما بعد البلوغ بالسن إذا لم يظهر به علامات يستدل بها على كونه رجلاً أو امرأة ويكره لبس الحرير أيضاً قال : وأكره له أن ينكشف قدام الرجال أو قدام النساء. ومعناه إذا كان قد راهق. فإن قلت : وهل يكره أن يخلو به رجل أجنبي ليس بمحرم منه أو يخلو هو بامرأة أجنبية ليس بمحرم منها؟ قلت : نعم إذا خلا بالخنثى رجل محرم منه فلا بأس، وكذلك الخنثى إذا خلا بامرأة هو محرم منها. ولا يسافر الخنثى بامرأة . غیر محرم منه ولا بأس أن يسافر الخنثى مع محرم من الرجال ثلاثة أيام ولياليها ولا يختنه رجل وامرأة لأن الخنثى أو صبية؛ فإن كان صبياً يجوز للرجال أن كنته، وإن كان مراهقاً يشتهي أو لا، وإن كان صبية فلا بأس للنساء أن تخنتها إذا كانت غير مراهقة لأنها لا تشتهي، وإذا كانت غير مراهقة وهي تشتهي أو لا، فإن قيل ما الفرق بين الحياة والموت حيث قلتم إذا مات الخنثى يميم بالصعيد، ولا يغسله رجل ولا امرأة ولم تقولوا أنه يشتري له جارية من ماله أو من مال أبيه أو من مال بيت المال إذا لم يكن لها مال ثم يبيعها الإمام بعد ما غسلته ويرد ثمنها إلى بيت المال؟ قلنا : شراء الجارية بعد موت الخنثى لتغسله لا تفيد إباحة الغسل لأنه لا يملكها الخنثى ولا يبقى على ملكه لحاجة الغسل، فأما ما دام حياً فهو من أهل الملك لأنه رجل أو امرأة فيملك الجارية التي اشتريت له، وإذا ملك الجارية التي اشتريت له كان شراء الجارية يفيد إباحة الختان. قال رحمه الله : (وتبتاع له أمة تختنه يعني بماله لأنه يجوز لمملوكه النظر إليه مطلقاً إن كان ذكراً، وللضرورة إن كان أنثى، ويكره أن يخنته رجل لاحتمال أنه ذكر أو امرأة لاحتمال أنه أنثى فكان الاحتياط فيما ذكرنا لأنه لا يحرم على تقدير أن يكون ذكراً وعلى تقدير أن يكون أنثى لأن نظر الجنس إلى الجنس أخف
Book of the Hermaphrodite. If he is a free adult, it is obligatory upon him that. And he sits in his prayer like the sitting of a woman, because if he is a man, he has left a sunnah, and that is permissible in general, and if he is a woman, then she has committed a disliked act by sitting like men. And the principle regarding that which relates to acts of worship: Muhammad said: I prefer that he pray with a head covering due to the possibility that he is a woman, meaning before puberty. If he prays without it, then if he is not mature, he is not ordered to repeat except by istihsan out of caution and consideration. In al-Hidayah: He prayed without a head covering, a woman should repeat, and that is istihsan. This is if the hermaphrodite is adolescent but not yet mature. If he is mature, meaning he has reached puberty by age and no signs of men or women appear, his prayer without a head covering is not valid if the hermaphrodite is free. In al-Sinaghaki and some copies: If he is mature and prayed without a head covering, a woman, then he should repeat, and this is by way of precaution. Thus is the wording of al-Mabsut. And he did not address whether the way of precaution is on the basis of recommendation or obligation, and the apparent is obligation. He said: And he sits in his prayer like the sitting of a woman. If this hermaphrodite enters ihram while he is adolescent but not yet mature and it is not clear whether he is a woman, Abu Yusuf said: I have no knowledge regarding his clothing. And Muhammad said: If he wears sewn clothes, that would be more precautionary due to the possibility that she is female, as it is not permissible for her to uncover her private parts. He said: And it is disliked for him to wear jewelry. And he intended by that after reaching puberty by age, when no signs appear by which one can deduce whether he is a man or woman. And wearing silk is also disliked. He said: And I dislike for him to be uncovered in front of men or in front of women. And its meaning is when he is adolescent. If you say: Is it disliked for a man who is not a mahram to be alone with him, or for him to be alone with a woman who is not his mahram? I say: Yes. If a man who is a mahram is alone with the hermaphrodite, then there is no problem, and likewise the hermaphrodite if he is alone with a woman who is his mahram. And the hermaphrodite should not travel with a woman who is not his mahram, and there is no problem for the hermaphrodite to travel with a male mahram for three days and nights. And a man or woman should not circumcise him, because the hermaphrodite is a boy or a girl. If he is a boy, it is permissible for men to circumcise him, whether he is adolescent and desires or not. If he is a girl, there is no problem for women to circumcise her if she is not adolescent because she does not desire. If she is not adolescent, whether she desires or not. If it is said: What is the difference between life and death, where you say if the hermaphrodite dies, he is performed tayammum with earth, and no man or woman washes him, and you do not say that a slave girl is bought for him from his wealth or from his father's wealth or from the public treasury if he has no wealth, then the imam sells her after she washes him and returns her price to the public treasury? We say: Buying a slave girl after the death of the hermaphrodite to wash him does not make washing permissible because the hermaphrodite does not own her, and she does not remain in his ownership for the need of washing. As for while he is alive, he is capable of ownership because he is a man or a woman, so he owns the slave girl bought for him. And when he owns the slave girl bought for him, the purchase of the slave girl makes circumcision permissible. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (A female slave is bought for him to circumcise him) meaning from his wealth, because his slave is permitted to look at him absolutely if he is male, and out of necessity if she is female. And it is disliked for a man to circumcise him due to the possibility that he is a man or a woman, due to the possibility that she is female. So the precaution is in what we mentioned, because it is not forbidden on the assumption that he is a man, and on the assumption that she is a woman, because the look of one gender to the other is lighter.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, deals with the rulings specific to a hermaphrodite (khuntha) whose gender is ambiguous. The discussion covers prayer, dress, seclusion, travel, and circumcision, all governed by the principle of precaution (ihtiyat) due to the uncertainty of gender. The author begins by stating that a free adult hermaphrodite must pray sitting like a woman. This is because if the khuntha is actually a man, sitting like a woman means he has left a sunnah (recommended act), which is permissible. But if the khuntha is actually a woman, sitting like a man would be makruh (disliked). Thus the precautionary sitting is like a woman. Regarding covering the head (qina') during prayer, Imam Muhammad said it is preferable for a khuntha approaching puberty to pray with a head covering because of the possibility that he is female. If a khuntha who is not yet mature prays without a head covering, he is not ordered to repeat the prayer except by istihsan (juristic preference) as a cautious approach. Al-Hidayah states that if a woman prays without a head covering, she must repeat, and this is istihsan. For a mature khuntha (by age) with no signs of gender, the prayer without a head covering is not valid (la tujzihi) if the khuntha is free. Some sources say he must repeat by way of precaution. The author notes that it is unclear whether this precaution is on the level of mustahabb (recommended) or wajib (obligatory), and the apparent view is that it is wajib. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding what a khuntha should wear when entering ihram. Abu Yusuf said he has no knowledge about the clothing, while Muhammad said wearing sewn clothes is more precautionary because of the possibility that the khuntha is female, for whom covering the private parts is obligatory. Wearing jewelry and silk is also disliked. Seclusion with a non-mahram is disliked, but with a mahram it is fine. The khuntha should not travel with a non-mahram woman, but can travel with a male mahram for three days. Rulings on circumcision: the khuntha should not be circumcised by a man or a woman due to the possibility of the opposite gender. If the khuntha is a boy, men may circumcise him; if a girl, women may circumcise her, provided she is not adolescent. The passage then addresses a question: why for death do we use tayammum and not washing, while for life we allow buying a slave for circumcision? The answer is that a dead person cannot own, so a bought slave does not make washing permissible; but a living person can own, so buying a slave for circumcision is valid. The matn (said by the author of Kanz) states that a female slave is bought for the khuntha to perform circumcision using his wealth, because the slave can look at him regardless of gender — absolutely if he is male, and out of necessity if female. It is disliked for a man to circumcise him due to gender ambiguity. The precaution is that the slave circumcises, as it is not haram on either assumption.

Key Terms
الخنثى (al-khuntha): A person whose gender is ambiguous, possessing both or no clear male/female characteristics.
قناع (qina'): A head covering worn during prayer, obligatory for women, sunnah for men.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyat): The principle of legal precaution, choosing the safer option to avoid potential sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a khuntha sitting in prayer?
A) He must sit like a man.
B) He must sit like a woman.
C) He may choose.
D) He should sit like a woman as a precaution.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rulings for the khuntha are built on ihtiyat (precaution) to avoid potential sin, requiring the khuntha to adopt the more restrictive gender-specific practice when unsure.

The Deaf-Mute's Gesture and Writing as Valid Expression
Explains the Hanafi view that a deaf-mute's gesture and writing are like speech, refuting al-Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the validity of gesture for a deaf-mute vs. a tongue-tied person, state the conditions under which gesture is equivalent to speech, and identify the exceptions (hudud) and the three categories of writing.

Core Idea
A deaf-mute's habitual gesture is like speech for all rulings except hudud; the tongue-tied only after prolonged incapacity.

This passage addresses a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the legal status of communication by a person who cannot speak. The Hanafi school holds that a deaf-mute's habitual gesture and writing are equivalent to speech for most transactions, but not for hudud penalties. The passage also compares the deaf-mute to a temporarily tongue-tied person and discusses the evidentiary value of different types of writing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the schools' positions
State the Hanafi position that a deaf-mute's gesture and writing are like speech (except hudud) and that a tongue-tied person is initially different. Contrast with al-Shafi'i who sees no difference.
The dispute is over whether temporary impediment is equivalent to permanent.
2
Determine the condition for validity
The Hanafis require that the gesture be habitual (معهودة) and that the incapacity be permanent or prolonged. For the tongue-tied, only after a period (1-6 months) does the condition become like that of a deaf-mute.
Duration estimates vary; the fatwa follows the narration from Abu Hanifa that it is valid if the condition continues until death.
3
Analyze the evidence
The primary proof is qiyas: if a capable person's gesture can serve as expression, then the incapable is more deserving. The author also cites the example of the Prophet explaining the month by gesture.
The evidence is categorical but the author notes a logical gap: the same reasoning would make writing valid for hudud, but the school's established position is otherwise.
4
Examine the categories of writing
Writing is divided into three levels: (1) clear and formally signed – valid as proof; (2) clear but unsigned – void unless corroborated; (3) not clear (on air/water) – no effect.
Custom (urf) and clarity determine evidentiary weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الخنثى وطلاق وبيع وشراء وقود[؟] وقال الشافعي: لا فرق بين معتقل اللسان والأخرس. ولنا أن الإشارة إنما تقوم مقام العبارة إذا صارت معهودة وذلك في الأخرس دون معتقل اللسان حتى لو امتد ذلك وصارت إشارته معهودة صار بمنزلة الأخرس. وقدر مدة الامتداد في المحيط بشهر، وفي جامع الفصولين بستة أشهر وقدر التمرتاشي الامتداد بستة، وذكر الحاكم أبو محمد رواية عن أبي حنيفة فقال: إذا دامت العقلة إلى وقت الموت يجوز إقراره بالإشارة ويجوز الإشهاد عليه لأن عجز عن النطق بمعنى لا يرجى زواله فكان كالأخرس . قل: وعليه الفتوى وأطلق في الأخرس فشمل الأصلي والعارض، والمراد الأصلي. أما الوصية لأن التقصير جاء من قبله حيث أخر الوصية إلى هذا الوقت بخلاف الأخرس لأنه لا تفريط من جهته. ولأن العارض على شرف الزوال دون الأصل فلا يقاس أحدهما على الآخر. وإذا كان إيماء الأخرس وكتابته كالبيان وهو النطق باللسان تلزمه الأحكام بالإشارة والكتابة حتى يجوز نكاحه وطلاقه وعتقه وبيعه وشراؤه إلى غير ذلك من الأحكام لأن الإشارة تكون بياناً من القادر على النطق فالعاجز أولى، ولأنه بين الشهر بالإشارة حيث قال: الشهر هكذا وأشار بأصابعه قالوا والكتاب ممن يأتي بمنزلة الخطاب ممن ذكر. أقول: فيه شيء وهو أن هذا يدل على بعض المدعي ولا يدل على بعضه الآخر بل يدل على خلافه فإن كتابة الأخرس حجة فيما سوى الحدود وليس بحجة في الحدود، وهذا الدليل المذكور لا يدل على عدم كونها حجة في الحدود إذ لا فرق فيه بين الحدود وما سواها بل يدل على كونها حجة في الحدود أيضاً إذا كانت مستبينة مرسومة وهو بمنزلة النطق في الغائب والحاضر على ما قالوا فإنه إذا كان بمنزلة النطق في حق الحاضر أيضاً لم يكن حجة ضرورة فينبغي أن يكون حجة في الحدود أيضاً كما كان النطق حجة فيها فليتأمل في المخلص. والدليل على أن الدلالة كالبيان هو أنه بلغ الرسالة بالكتاب كالخطاب فإذا كان خطاباً في حق القادر ففي حق الأخرس أولى لأن عجزه ظاهر وألزم عادة لأن الغائب يقدر على الحضور بل يقدر ظاهراً والأخرس لا يقدر على نطق والظاهر بقاؤه على الدوام. ثم الكتاب على ثلاثة مراتب: مستبين أن يكون معنوناً أي مصدراً بالعنوان وهو أن يكتب في صدره من ومرسوم وهو فلان على ما جرت به العادة في سير الكتب فيكون هذا كالنطق فيلزم حجة، ومستبين غير مرسوم كالكتابة على الجدران وأوراق الأشجار أو على الكاغض لا على وجه الرسم فإن هذا يكون لغواً لأنه لا عرف في إظهار الأمر بهذا الطريق فلا يكون حجة إلا بانضمام شيء آخر إليه كالبينة والإشهاد عليه والإملاء على الغير حتى يكتبه لأن الكتابة قد تكون تجربة وقد تكون للتحقيق، وبهذه الإشارة تتبين الجهة وقيل الإملاء من غير إشهاد لا يكون حجة والأول أظهر. وغير مستبين كالكتابة على الهواء أو الماء وهو بمنزلة كلام غير مسموع ولا يثبت به شيء من الأحكام وإن نوى.
Book of the Hermaphrodite. And divorce, sale, purchase, and [agency?]... Al-Shafi'i said: There is no difference between one with a tied tongue and a deaf-mute. Our position: Gesture takes the place of speech only when it becomes habitual, and that applies to the deaf-mute, not the tongue-tied. However, if [the tongue-tied condition] extends and his gesture becomes habitual, he becomes like a deaf-mute. The duration of extension is estimated in al-Muhit as one month, in Jami' al-Fusulayn as six months, and al-Tamurtashi estimated six [months]. Al-Hakim Abu Muhammad narrated from Abu Hanifa: When the tying continues until death, his acknowledgment by gesture is valid, and witnessing against him is valid because he is unable to speak in a way unlikely to be removed, so he is like a deaf-mute. He said: And upon it is the fatwa. He spoke generally about the deaf-mute, including the congenital and the accidental, but the intended is the congenital. As for the will, because the shortcoming came from him by delaying the will to that time, unlike the deaf-mute, who has no negligence. And because the accidental is on the verge of disappearing unlike the congenital, so one cannot be analogized to the other. When the deaf-mute's gesture and writing are like expression—that is, oral speech—rulings are binding upon him by gesture and writing, even his marriage, divorce, manumission, sale, purchase, etc., because gesture is expression from one able to speak, so the incapable is more deserving. And because he explained the month by gesture: 'The month is like this,' pointing with his fingers. They said: The writing from one who is absent is equivalent to speech from one who is present. I say: There is something here: this evidence supports part of the claim but not the other part; rather it contradicts the other part, for the writing of a deaf-mute is proof in matters other than hadd penalties, not proof in hadd penalties. Yet this mentioned evidence does not indicate that it is not proof in hadd penalties, since it makes no distinction between hadd and other matters; rather it indicates that it is proof in hadd penalties as well if clearly written and signed, and it is like speech of the absent and present as they said. Since it is like speech also regarding the present, it would necessarily be proof, so it should be proof in hadd penalties as well, just as speech is proof in them. Let one reflect on the solution. The evidence that indication is like expression is that he delivered the message by writing as by speech. So if it is speech in the case of the capable, then in the case of the deaf-mute it is more deserving, because his incapacity is apparent and customary necessity arises because the absent person can attend—rather, he is apparently able—while the deaf-mute cannot speak, and the apparent assumption is that it remains permanently. Then writing is of three levels: clear: that it is addressed i.e. prefaced with an address, that he writes at its beginning 'from' and signed 'so-and-so' as is customary in correspondence—this is like speech and is proof; clear but not signed, like writing on walls, tree leaves, or on paper without official form—this is void because there is no custom of expressing matters in this way, so it is not proof unless accompanied by something else like evidence of witnesses witnessing it, or dictation so that another writes it, because writing may be practice or for verification. By this gesture the matter becomes clear. It was said dictation without witnesses is not proof, but the first is more apparent. And not clear, like writing on air or water—it is like unheard speech, and no ruling is established by it even if he intended.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing the ruling on the legal capacity of a deaf-mute (al-akhras) versus a temporarily tongue-tied person (mu'taqil al-lisan). The Hanafi position, opposed by al-Shafi'i, is that only the habitual gesture of the deaf-mute substitutes for speech. The author presents multiple time estimates for when a tongue-tied person becomes like a deaf-mute, and notes a narration from Abu Hanifa that if the condition lasts until death, his gesture is valid. He clarifies that the deaf-mute referred to is the congenital one, not the temporary, because the latter may recover. The commentary then discusses the evidentiary value of gesture and writing: they are equivalent to speech for all rulings except hudud. The author critically observes that the general evidence (absence makes writing like speech) would suggest writing is proof even in hudud, but the school's established position is that it is not. He explains the three categories of writing: (1) clear and formally addressed/signed – valid as proof; (2) clear but not formally signed – void unless corroborated by other evidence; (3) not clear (e.g., on air/water) – no effect. This lesson thus establishes the Hanafi framework for evaluating the expression of those unable to speak.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is permanently unable to speak, either congenital or from a non-recoverable condition.
معتقل اللسان (mu'taqil al-lisan): A person with a temporary speech impediment (tongue-tied) that is expected to be resolved.
الإشارة (al-isharah): Gesture, bodily indication used by a deaf-mute to communicate meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The gesture is never valid, no matter how long the condition lasts.
B) The gesture becomes valid immediately, without any duration requirement.
C) The gesture becomes valid only after the condition extends and becomes habitual; estimates range from one to six months.
D) The gesture is valid only if the condition lasts exactly six months.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a deaf-mute's habitual gesture and clear writing as equivalent to speech for all transactions except hudud, while a tongue-tied person must first demonstrate prolonged incapacity for his gesture to be accepted.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Can the Sultan Assign Kharaj or 'Ushr to the Land Owner?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the Sultan's authority over kharaj and 'ushr assignment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the Sultan's authority to assign kharaj and 'ushr, and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Kharaj can be assigned by the sultan, 'ushr cannot – based on legal nature: kharaj is a land owner's right, 'ushr is a right of the poor.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a rare intra-Hanafi disagreement among the three founding imams. While Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider both taxes as rights of the Muslim community, Abu Yusuf draws a critical distinction based on the legal nature of each. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) adopts Abu Yusuf's view, which has practical implications for state tax policy and religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the overall Hanafi position
Identify that there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement. The passage presents two views: Abu Yusuf vs. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Both views are within the Hanafi school, but the fatwa adopts one.
2
Identify the variant within the Hanafi school
Determine which imam holds which view. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say neither tax can be assigned; Abu Yusuf says only kharaj can be assigned.
Abu Yusuf distinguishes between kharaj and 'ushr based on their legal nature.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil) of each position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad argue both are for the community treasury (bayt al-mal). Abu Yusuf argues kharaj is a right of the land owner because it is a tax on his property, while 'ushr is akin to zakat – a specific right of the poor.
The key difference is whether the tax is considered a right of the owner or of the poor.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, the Sultan can grant the owner the kharaj revenue, incentivizing cultivation. Under the other view, the owner must pay and the state collects both.
This affects land tax policies and treatment of agricultural produce.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The passage states 'And the fatwa is upon the statement of Abu Yusuf.' So the operative ruling is that the Sultan may assign kharaj but not 'ushr.
This tarjih is from the later Hanafi tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلطان جعل الخراج لرب الأرض جاز وإن جعل العشر لا. وهذا عند أبي يوسف. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد : لا يجوز فيهما لأنهما في جماعة المسلمين . ولأبي يوسف صاحب الخراج له حق في الخراج فصح تركه عليه وهو الإمام والعشر حق الفقراء على الخلوص كالزكاة فلا يجوز تركه عليه وعلى قول أبي يوسف الفتوى .
If the Sultan makes the kharaj (land tax) for the land owner, it is permissible; and if he makes the 'ushr (tithe), it is not. This is according to Abu Yusuf. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: It is not permissible in either case because they are in the community of Muslims. According to Abu Yusuf, the owner of the kharaj has a right in the kharaj, so his relinquishing it is valid, and he is the Imam; and the 'ushr is the right of the poor specifically, like zakat, so relinquishing it is not valid. And the fatwa is upon the statement of Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the Sultan's authority to assign land taxes to the property owner. The issue is whether the Sultan can grant the land owner the right to collect the kharaj (land tax) or the 'ushr (tithe) from the land, effectively exempting him or giving him that revenue. According to Abu Yusuf, the Sultan can assign the kharaj to the owner because the owner has a right in the kharaj (as it is a tax on the land he owns), so it can be waived. However, Abu Yusuf says the 'ushr cannot be assigned because it is like zakat—a specific right of the poor that cannot be given to the land owner. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree entirely: the Sultan cannot assign either because both taxes are due to the Muslim community (the treasury), not to the individual owner. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf. This reflects the Hanafi view that the state has authority over kharaj but 'ushr is a religious obligation for the poor.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharaj): A land tax imposed on agricultural land, collected by the state.
العشر (al-'ushr): The tithe (one-tenth) on agricultural produce from lands cultivated by Muslims, akin to zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), may the Sultan assign the 'ushr to the land owner?
A) Yes, because it is also a land tax.
B) No, because it is like zakat, a right of the poor.
C) Yes, according to all Hanafi imams.
D) No, because the land owner has no right in any tax.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between kharaj (permissible to assign) and 'ushr (not permissible) based on the nature of the tax, with Abu Yusuf's view being the mufta bihi.
