Ruling on Leasing or Selling Land for Kharaj When Owners Cannot Farm
Ruling on state intervention for kharaj collection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the Imam's authority to lease or sell land for kharaj when the owner is unable to farm, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The Imam can lease or sell land to preserve the kharaj right, balancing private ownership with public obligation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a fiscal dilemma: what happens when landowners cannot cultivate their kharaj land and the state's right to tax is at risk? The Hanafi school permits the Imam to intervene by leasing or selling the land, ensuring the kharaj continues to fund the public treasury. The ruling is grounded in qiyas on debts attached to property, and it includes a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement about the judge's power to sell directly versus ordering the owner to sell. Understanding this ruling reveals how Islamic law prioritizes collective obligations without extinguishing private ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action the Imam is taking and its legal classification. The passage states that leasing or selling owned land when the owner cannot farm or pay kharaj is permissible (ja'iz).
The ruling applies to the Imam, not the owner; he acts as a public authority.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the evidence cited. The proof is qiyas: the right of kharaj is attached to the land itself, like the debt of a slave permitted to trade or the debt of a deceased person's estate, where the judge can sell property to settle the debt.
The qiyas is based on the illah of 'right attached to property' (ta'alluq al-haqq bi al-raqabah).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the proof yields the ruling. Since kharaj is attached to the land, the Imam can sell the land to recover it, just as a judge sells a slave or an estate to satisfy debts.
This analogy overrides the general principle that a judge cannot seize assets for debt, due to public interest.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) allow the Imam to sell directly. Abu Hanifa's earlier view is that the Imam should order the owner to sell, but later narrations (Nawadir) show he also approved alternative measures like cultivating from the treasury.
The passage presents both views but favors the Sahibayn's position as the mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
Determine which view is relied upon and why. The Sahibayn's view is adopted because of public benefit (maslahah 'ammah) and the attachment of kharaj to the land. The passage also notes that Abu Hanifa's later narrations align with this approach.
The tarjih is supported by the qiyas and the principle that public need overrides private harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو دفع الأراضي المملوكة إلى قوم ليعطوا الخراج جاز) معناه أن أصحاب الأراضي إذا عجزوا عن زراعة الأرض وأداء الخراج دفع الإمام الأراضي إلى غيرهم بالأجرة أي يؤاجر الأراضي للقادرين على الزراعة ويأخذ الخراج من أجرتها، فإن فضل شيء من أجرتها يدفع إلى أربابها وهم الملاك لأنه لا وجه لإزالة ملكهم بغير رضاهم من غير ضرورة ولا وجه إلى تعطيل حق المقاتلة فتعين ما ذكرنا، فإن لم يجد من يستأجرها باعها الإمام لمن يقدر على الزراعة لأنه إذا لم يبعها يفوت حق المقاتلة في الخراج أصلاً. ولو باع يفوت حق المالك في العين والفوات إلى خلف كلا فوات فيبيع تخفيفاً للنظر من الجانبين وليس له أن يملكها غيرهم بغير عوض، وإذا باعها يأخذ الخراج الماضي من الثمن إذا كان عليهم خراج ورد الفضل إلى أصحابها. ثم قيل : هذا قول أبي يوسف ومحمد لأن عندهما القاضي يملك مال المديون بالدين والنفقة وعند أبي حنيفة لا يملك ذلك فلا يبيعها لكن يأمر صاحبها ببيع ببيعها. وقيل: هذا قول الكل والفرق لأبي حنيفة بين هذا وبين غيره من الديون أن في هذا ضرراً خاصاً ونفعاً عاماً والنفع العام مقدم على الضرر الخاص، ولأن الخراج متعلق برقبة الأرض فصار كدين العبد المأذون له في التجارة ودين الميت في التركة فإن القاضي يملك البيع فيهما لتعلق الحق بالرقبة فكذا هذا. وذكر في النوادر عن أبي حنيفة أن أهل الخراج إذا هربوا إن شاء الإمام عمرها من بيت المال والغلة للمسلمين، وإن شاء دفع إلى قوم وأطعمهم على شيء إذ كأنه ما يأخذ للمسلمين لأن فيه حفظ الخراج على المسلمين والملك على أربابها، فإذا عمرها من بيت المال يكون بقدر ما ينفق في عمارتها قرضاً لأن الإمام مأمور بتهيئة بين المال بأي وجه يتهيأ له
And if he gives the owned lands to a group so that they give the kharaj, it is permissible. Its meaning is that when the owners of the lands are unable to cultivate the land and pay the kharaj, the Imam gives the lands to others for rent, i.e., he leases the lands to those able to cultivate and takes the kharaj from their rent. If anything remains from the rent, he gives it to the owners (the proprietors) because there is no justification for removing their ownership without their consent without necessity, and there is no justification for nullifying the right of the fighters (muqatila). So what we mentioned is determined. If he does not find anyone to lease it, the Imam sells it to someone able to cultivate, because if he does not sell it, the right of the fighters in the kharaj is lost entirely. If he sells, the right of the owner in the property is lost, and the loss is to both sides; so he sells as a facility to consider both sides. He is not allowed to give it to others without compensation. When he sells, he takes the past kharaj from the price if there was a kharaj due on them, and returns the surplus to the owners. Then it is said: this is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad because they hold that the judge can take possession of the property of a debtor for debt and maintenance; according to Abu Hanifa, he does not have that power, so he does not sell but commands the owner to sell. It is also said: this is the statement of all, and the distinction for Abu Hanifa between this and other debts is that here there is private harm and public benefit, and public benefit takes precedence over private harm; and because the kharaj is attached to the land itself, so it becomes like the debt of a slave permitted to trade and the debt of a deceased person in the estate, for the judge can sell in those cases due to the right being attached to the property; so here too. It is mentioned in al-Nawadir from Abu Hanifa that if the kharaj payers flee, the Imam may cultivate it from the treasury and the produce is for the Muslims, or he may give it to a group and feed them for something, as if he takes for the Muslims because in it is preservation of the kharaj for the Muslims and ownership for the owners. If he cultivates it from the treasury, it is as a loan to the extent of what he spends on cultivation, because the Imam is ordered to secure the wealth in any way possible.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on state intervention when landowners are unable to cultivate their land and pay the kharaj (land tax). The Imam is authorized to lease or sell the land to those who can farm it, ensuring the kharaj is paid to maintain the state's fiscal rights (for the 'fighters' or public treasury). The proceeds from rent are used for kharaj, and any surplus goes to the owner. If no lessee is found, sale is permitted to avoid complete loss of kharaj. The passage then discusses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow the judge to sell the land directly, while Abu Hanifa prefers ordering the owner to sell, because the judge generally cannot seize assets for debt. However, a public-interest exception is made here, and a narration from al-Nawadir shows Abu Hanifa also allowed alternative measures like the Imam cultivating the land from the treasury. The ruling balances private ownership with public obligation of kharaj.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax paid by landowners to the state, even if they do not cultivate.
المقاتلة (al-muqatilah): The army or fighters who are entitled to the kharaj revenue.
تعلق الحق بالرقبة (ta'alluq al-haqq bi al-raqabah): Attachment of a right (like debt) to the property itself, not just the person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Ruling of a Persian Oath Phrase as Iqrar
Ruling that a Persian phrase constitutes acknowledgment of an oath by Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the Persian phrase 'bar man sawkand ast ke in kar nakunam' is an iqrar of an oath by Allah and identify the consequence of breaking it.

Core Idea
A Persian oath phrase is a binding oath by Allah — an iqrar, not mere speech.

This lesson examines a specific Persian phrase that is legally considered an acknowledgment (iqrar) of an oath by Allah. Even though the phrase does not explicitly mention Allah's name, the Hanafi school treats it as a valid oath. Understanding this ruling helps believers avoid unintentionally incurring the obligation of kaffarah (expiation) for breaking such an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Phrase
Locate the exact Persian phrase: 'bar man sawkand ast ke in kar nakunam'.
The phrase is given in the source text with Arabic commentary.
2
Understand the Translation
Parse the meaning: 'bar man sawkand ast' = 'an oath is upon me'; 'in kar nakunam' = 'I will not do this action'.
The commentator clarifies each Persian word.
3
Recognize as Iqrar
The phrase is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of having taken an oath by Allah. It is not merely a figure of speech.
Iqrar means admission of a legal fact.
4
Note the Legal Effect
If the stated action is performed (breaking the oath), kaffarah (expiation) becomes obligatory — feeding ten poor people or fasting three days.
Hanafi fiqh: breaking a valid oath requires kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال شخص بر من سوکند است که يعني على اليمين. قال رحمه الله : (ولو قال أين كار يعني هذا الفعل (نكنم) يعني لا أفعل (فهذا إقرار باليمين بالله تعالى
And if a person said 'bar man sawkand ast ke', meaning 'upon me is an oath'. He (the author) said: (And if he said 'in kar' meaning 'this action' (nakunam) meaning 'I will not do', then this is an iqrar of an oath by Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage deals with oath expressions. If someone says 'there is an oath upon me that I will not do this action', it is considered an acknowledgment (iqrar) that he has sworn an oath by Allah. Consequently, if he breaks that promise, he must perform kaffarah (expiation) for breaking an oath. The commentator clarifies the Persian words: 'bar man sawkand ast' means 'an oath is upon me', and 'in kar nakunam' means 'I will not do this action'. This is treated as a valid oath, not just a figure of speech.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): Oath; here, an oath by Allah.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment; here, admitting that one has taken an oath.

Key Takeaway
The Persian phrase 'bar man sawkand ast ke in kar nakunam' is an iqrar of an oath by Allah, making it a binding oath that requires kaffarah if broken.

Oath for Claimant When Acknowledger Claims Lying
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath for claimant after acknowledgment of lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the claimant must swear when the acknowledger claims he lied, and apply the mufta bihi position using istihsan.

Core Idea
When an acknowledger claims he lied, the claimant must swear – a Hanafi departure from strict proof due to custom.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute within the chapter of acknowledgment (iqrar). Normally, an acknowledgment is a binding proof, but here the school diverges due to prevalent custom. The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that no oath is needed because acknowledgment is like decisive evidence. However, Abu Yusuf, followed by the later fatwa, requires the claimant to swear, based on istihsan (juristic preference) and changing social conditions. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance principles with practical realities.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi mufta bihi position
Identify the ruling that is operative for fatwa: the claimant must swear that the acknowledger was not lying.
This is Abu Yusuf's view, adopted by later scholars.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
State the Sahibayn's view (no oath) and their dalil: acknowledgment is a binding proof (hujjah mulzimah) like bayyinah; the likelihood of lying is remote because it harms the acknowledger.
The Sahibayn use qiyas: comparing acknowledgment to evidence that cannot be challenged without proof.
3
Follow the opponent's reasoning
Understand why the Sahibayn consider the oath unnecessary: if acknowledgment is conclusive, adding an oath is redundant and undermines its force.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (istihsan)
Examine the basis of istihsan: custom (urf) shows that people often write documents before receiving money, so acknowledgment may not represent a true debt. Also, due to increased deceit, an oath is a safeguard.
The Hanafi response does not reject the principle of hujjah mulzimah but limits it by custom.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Practical consequence: Under Sahibayn, the claimant never swears and the denial is ignored. Under the mufta bihi, the claimant must swear to claim successfully. The tarjih is based on 'alayhi al-fatwa' due to changing times.
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) explicitly states that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقر بدين أو غيره ثم قال كنت كاذبا فيما أقررت حلف المقر له أن المقر ما كان كاذبا فيما أقر به ولست بمبطل فيما أدعيه عليه والإقرار ليس بسبب للملك) وهذا قول أبي يوسف. وقالا : لا يحلف لأن الإقرار حجة ملزمة شرعاً فلا يصار معه إلى اليمين كالبينة بل أولى لأن احتمال الكذب فيه أبعد لتضرره بذلك، ووجه الاستحسان أن العادة جرت بين الناس أنهم يكتبون الصك إذا أرادوا الاستدانة قبل الأخذ ثم يأخذون المال فلا يكون الأقرار دليلاً، وكذا الوادعي وارث المقر يحلف المقر له على الصحيح لأن الوارث أدعى الجزء الذي في يد المقر له فاليمين على نفي العلم أننا لا نعلم أنه كاذب فيحلف وعليه الفتوى لتغير أحوال الناس وكثرة الخداع والخيانات وهو يتضرر بذلك والمدعي لا يضره اليمين إن كان صادقاً فيصار إليه .
If a person acknowledges a debt or something else and then says, 'I was lying in what I acknowledged,' the one in whose favor the acknowledgment was made is made to swear that the acknowledger was not lying in what he acknowledged, and that he (the claimant) is not invalidating what he claims against him. And acknowledgment is not a cause for ownership. This is the statement of Abu Yusuf. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) said: He is not made to swear because an acknowledgment is a binding proof legally, so an oath is not resorted to with it, like a decisive evidence, rather it is more so because the possibility of lying in it is more remote due to his harming himself by it. The basis of istihsan is that the custom among people is that they write a document when they want to borrow before taking and then take the money, so the acknowledgment is not evidence. Similarly, if the deceased left a depositor or an heir of the acknowledger, the claimant is made to swear according to the correct view, because the heir claims the portion that is in the hand of the claimant, so the oath is on negation of knowledge: 'We do not know that he is lying.' So he swears, and the fatwa is upon this due to the change in the conditions of people and the prevalence of deception and betrayals, and he (the acknowledger) is harmed by that, while the claimant is not harmed by the oath if he is truthful, so it is resorted to.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute within the chapter of acknowledgment (iqrar). The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) rules that if a person acknowledges a debt then claims he was lying, the claimant is made to take an oath. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) first attributes this view to Abu Yusuf. The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that acknowledgment is a binding proof (hujjah mulzimah) like a decisive evidence, so no oath is needed; in fact, they say the likelihood of lying in an acknowledgment that harms oneself is even more remote. However, the commentator explains that the Hanafi school takes the position of istihsan based on the common practice of people writing documents before actually receiving money. In modern times (for the author's era), due to widespread deception, the ruling is that the claimant is made to swear that he does not know the acknowledger was lying. This is the mufta bihi (operative fatwa). The passage also extends this to the case of a depositor or heir of the acknowledger, where the claimant is likewise made to swear.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or debt by a person against himself, which is normally a binding proof.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by the claimant that the acknowledger was not lying.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference: deviating from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a ruling that better serves justice or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in this dispute, what happens when a person acknowledges a debt and then claims he was lying?
A) The acknowledgment is invalidated and no oath is required.
B) The claimant must swear that the acknowledger was not lying.
C) The acknowledger is believed without oath.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mufta bihi requires the claimant to swear when the acknowledger claims lying, based on istihsan and custom.

Silence as Acceptance in Agency and Analogous Contracts
Ruling that silence constitutes acceptance in agency and analogous contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that silence constitutes acceptance in agency and debt gift contracts, and apply the analogy to similar transactions.

Core Idea
Silence on hearing an offer indicates acceptance by custom; the law respects customary practice.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a key principle: in certain contracts, immediate silence after an offer is taken as acceptance. The proof is not a revealed text but customary practice (urf), and the ruling is extended by analogy from gift of debt to agency and, with nuance, to waqf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is responding to an offer of agency by silence. The ruling: it constitutes acceptance and agency is established.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is customary practice (urf) and analogy (qiyas) to the case of gift of debt.
The author explicitly mentions custom as the basis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Custom shows that immediate silence indicates consent, as people normally speak up if they disagree.
The phrase 'دليل القبول عادة' is key.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Regarding waqf, Al-Ansari reports that Abu Hanifa held that acceptance is not required, so silence vs rejection is irrelevant; the waqf is effective upon pronouncement.
This is an intra-Hanafi nuance.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The commentator favors the view that waqf does not require acceptance, consistent with Abu Hanifa's position.
The mufta bihi is that waqf is effective upon pronouncement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو قال لآخر وكلتك ببيع هذا فسكت صار وكيلا لأن سكوته وعدم رده من ساعته دليل القبول عادة ونظيره هبة الدين ممن عليه الدين فإنه إذا سكت صحت الهبة وسقط الدين لما بينا وإن قال من ساعته لا أقبل بطل وبقي الدين على حاله، وكذا لو قال جعلت أرضي عليك وقفاً فسكت صحت، ولو قال لا أقبل بطل. وقال الأنصاري : الوقف لا يبطل بقوله لا أقبل لأنه وقف الله تعالى والأشبه أن يكون هذا قول أبي حنيفة. يصير بمجرد قوله وقفت داري
If he says to another, 'I appoint you to sell this,' and he remains silent, he becomes an agent because his silence and lack of immediate refusal is evidence of acceptance by custom. Its parallel is the gift of a debt from the debtor: if he remains silent, the gift is valid and the debt is dropped, as we have explained, but if he says immediately 'I do not accept,' it becomes void and the debt remains as it was. Similarly, if he says, 'I have made my land an endowment (waqf) for you,' and he remains silent, it is valid, and if he says 'I do not accept,' it becomes void. Al-Ansari said: The waqf does not become void by him saying 'I do not accept' because it is the endowment of Allah, and it is more similar that this is the statement of Abu Hanifa. It becomes effective simply by his saying 'I have endowed my house.'
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage rules that silence in response to an offer of agency constitutes acceptance, based on custom. The analogy is drawn to a gift of debt: if the debtor remains silent when offered a gift of the debt, it is valid; if he immediately rejects, it is void. The same applies to waqf (endowment): silence indicates acceptance. However, Al-Ansari notes a difference of opinion: according to Abu Hanifa, waqf does not require acceptance from the beneficiary, so a verbal rejection does not nullify it. The commentator reconciles this, noting that the Hanafi position is that waqf is effective simply by the pronouncement of the endower, without need for acceptance.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): Agency: a contract where one person authorizes another to act on his behalf.
هبة الدين (hibat al-dayn): Gift of a debt: the creditor forgives the debt, which becomes effective upon acceptance by the debtor.
الوقـف (al-waqf): Endowment: dedicating a property for charitable purposes, which according to Abu Hanifa becomes effective immediately upon pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He does not become an agent because acceptance must be verbal.
B) He becomes an agent because silence indicates acceptance by custom.
C) He becomes an agent only if he nods.
D) The offer is void.

Key Takeaway
Immediate silence after an offer can constitute acceptance by custom in certain contracts like agency and gift of debt, but waqf is an exception where acceptance is not required.

Conditional Agency with Revocation
Ruling on a conditional agency with staggered revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a conditional agency suspended upon revocation and explain the sequence of effects of multiple revocations.

Core Idea
A conditional agency can be suspended on a condition; revocation of one agency activates the next.

Agency contracts in Hanafi fiqh can be made conditional (mu'allaqah). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a scenario where a principal creates two agencies: one immediate and one that activates upon revocation of the first. Understanding the sequence of revocations is crucial for correctly applying the rulings in practice, especially when the principal wishes to retract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two agencies
Recognize that the principal creates an immediate agency (al-wakalah al-munajazah) and a conditional agency (al-wakalah al-mu'allaqah) that is suspended on the condition of revocation of the first.
The conditional agency is expressed as 'whenever I revoke you, you are my agent'.
2
Understand the condition trigger
The condition is the revocation of the immediate agency. The moment the principal utters 'I revoke you' (first عزلتك), the immediate agency ends and the conditional one becomes effective.
The same utterance serves both to revoke and to activate.
3
Analyze the second revocation
A second utterance of 'I revoke you' (second عزلتك) revokes the newly activated agency, ending the relationship entirely.
The principal can also use alternative wording to retract both agencies simultaneously.
4
Distinguish the alternative formulation
If the principal says 'Whenever I appoint you, you are revoked' or uses 'كلما وكلتك فأنت معزول', the effect is that each appointment automatically triggers a revocation, achieving the same result but with different wording.
The commentator prefers the first formulation as more appropriate (أوجه).
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider a variant where the condition is not revocation but another event. The Hanafi rule allows suspension of agency on any valid condition, but the precise wording affects the legal consequences.
Always verify the exact phrasing used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلتك بكذا على إني متى عزلتك فأنت وكيلي يقول في عزله عزلتك ثم عزلتك أي ثم يقول عزلتك لأن الوكالة يجوز تعليقها بالشرط فيجوز تعليقها بالعزل عن الوكالة، فإن عزله انعزل عن الوكالة المنجزة ثم تنجزت المعلقة فصار توكيلاً جديداً، ثم بالعزل الثاني قد رجع عن الوكالة الثانية . قال رحمه الله : ولو قال كلما عزلتك فأنت وكيلي يقول رجعت عن الوكالة المعلقة وعزلت عن الوكالة المنجزة) وقيل يقول في عزله كلما وكلتك فأنت معزول لأنه كلما صار وكيلاً انعزل فيحصل مقصوده بذلك والأول أوجه .
I appoint you for such-and-such on condition that whenever I revoke you, you are my agent. (The explainer) says: In his revocation he says, 'I revoke you, then I revoke you,' meaning he then says 'I revoke you' because agency can be suspended upon a condition, so it can be suspended upon revocation of agency. If he revokes him, he is removed from the effective agency, then the suspended one takes effect, becoming a new appointment. Then by the second revocation, he has retracted from the second agency. And if he says, 'Whenever I revoke you, you are my agent,' he says, 'I have retracted from the suspended agency and revoked the effective agency.' And it is said that in his revocation he says, 'Whenever I appoint you, you are revoked' because whenever he becomes an agent, he is revoked, achieving his purpose with that. And the first is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage explains a complex case involving conditional agency. A person says to his agent: 'I appoint you for X, but whenever I revoke you, you become my agent again.' This creates two agencies: an immediate one and a suspended one that activates upon revocation. The first uttered revocation (عزلتك) serves to remove the agent from the first agency and simultaneously activate the second. A second utterance of 'I revoke you' then revokes the second agency. If the phrasing is 'Whenever I revoke you, you are my agent,' it means the speaker retracts both agencies. An alternative expression is to say 'Whenever I appoint you, you are revoked,' which achieves the same effect automatically. The commentator prefers the first formulation as more appropriate.

Key Terms
الوكالة المعلقة (al-wakalah al-mu'allaqah): Agency that is suspended upon a condition, such as revocation of another agency.
الوكالة المنجزة (al-wakalah al-munajazah): An immediate, unconditional agency.
العزل (al-azl): Revocation of agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal says, 'I appoint you, and whenever I revoke you, you are my agent,' what is the effect of the first revocation utterance?
A) It revokes both agencies simultaneously.
B) It revokes the immediate agency and activates the conditional one.
C) It only revokes the immediate agency; the conditional one is unaffected.
D) It creates a new agency without revoking the old one.

Key Takeaway
Conditional agency is valid in Hanafi fiqh; revocation of the immediate agency activates the suspended one, and a second revocation ends it.

Conciliation for a Minor: Claimant with Evidence
Ruling with proof: conditions for valid sulh by guardian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a guardian may validly make sulh on behalf of a minor when a third party has evidence, and apply the concept of taghabun (customary overlook).

Core Idea
Guardian can settle a claim with evidence for equivalent value or slightly more, because it secures the minor's asset.

When a third party claims a property from a minor and has valid evidence, the guardian may enter into conciliation to avoid losing the asset. The text specifies that the settlement must be for the equivalent value or within the range that people customarily overlook (taghabun), as this benefits the minor by preserving the asset. The principle is that the guardian must act in the minor's best interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
The passage begins with a third party (a man) claiming a house from a minor, and the father (guardian) makes conciliation (sulh) using the minor's property.
2
Check the claimant's evidence
The first condition: the claimant must have valid evidence (bayyinah). Only then does the possibility of valid conciliation arise.
If no evidence, see next lesson.
3
Examine the settlement amount
The conciliation amount must be equal to the value (bi-mithl al-qeemah) or slightly more within the range of taghabun (what people customarily overlook). This is because the minor benefits by avoiding losing the asset entirely.
More than taghabun would be harmful and invalid.
4
Understand the benefit rationale
The legal reason (ta'lil) is the benefit for the minor: the asset is secured. Without conciliation, the claimant would take it due to evidence.
Taghabun is allowed because exact valuation is often impossible.
5
Connect to the principle of guardianship
The underlying principle is wilaya nazariyya (guardianship based on consideration), meaning the guardian must act in the minor's best interest.
This principle is supported by the Quranic verse (6:152) cited later in the full passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدعى رجل دار فصالحه أبوه على مال الصبي فإن كان للمدعي بينة جاز إن كان بمثل القيمة أو أكثر مما يتغابن الناس فيه لأن للصبي فيه منفعة وهي سلامة العين له لأنه لو لم يصالح يستحقه المدعي فتنقد بالمثل وبقدر ما يتغابن فيه عادة لأنه لا يمكن التحرز عنه. قال رحمه الله : وإن لم يكن للمدعي بينة أو كانت غير عادلة لا لا يصح لأنه يكون متبرعاً بمال الصبي بالصلح لا مشترياً له لأنه لم يستحق المدعي شيئاً من ماله لو لا الصلح فلا منفعة للصبي في هذا الصلح بل فيه ضرر فلا يجوز لأن الولاية نظرية قال الله تعالى ولا تقربوا مال اليتيم إلا بالتي أحسن [الأنعام: 152] وإن كان الأب هو المدعي للصغير ولا بينة يجوز كيفما كان لأنه لم يثبت للصبي فيما أدعاه الأب له ملك ولا معنى الملك وهو التمكن من الأخذ فكان محصلاً له مالاً ، من غير أن يخرج من ملك الصبي شيئاً بمقابلته فكان نفعاً محضاً، فإن كان له بينة عادلة لا تجوز إلا بالمثل وبأقل لقدر ما يتغابن فيه لأنه صار في معنى الملك لتمكنه من الأخذ بالبينة العادلة، ووصي الأب في هذا كالأب لأنه قائم مقامه .
If a man claims a house and the father (of a minor) makes conciliation on behalf of the minor regarding the minor's property: if the claimant has evidence, it is permissible if it is for the equivalent value or more than what people normally overlook (mutaghābin), because there is a benefit for the minor in it, which is the safety of the asset for him; because if he did not make conciliation, the claimant would take it. So it is secure with the equivalent and with the amount customarily overlooked, because it cannot be avoided. He said: And if the claimant has no evidence or it is not just ('adila), it is not valid, because he would be donating the minor's property through the conciliation, not purchasing it, because the claimant was not entitled to anything of his property without the conciliation, so there is no benefit for the minor in this conciliation, rather there is harm, so it is not permissible because guardianship is based on consideration (nazariyya). Allah says: 'Do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner' (Quran 6:152). If the father himself is the claimant for the minor and has no evidence, it is permissible in any case, because he did not establish ownership for the minor in what he claims, nor the meaning of ownership, which is the ability to take. So he is acquiring property for him without anything leaving the minor's ownership in exchange, so it is pure benefit. If he has just evidence, it is not permissible except for the equivalent or less than what is customarily overlooked, because it has become in the meaning of ownership due to his ability to take by just evidence. The executor of the father is like the father in this because he stands in his place.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage details the conditions under which a guardian (father or executor) can enter into a conciliation (sulh) on behalf of a minor when a third party claims property. The key is the presence of benefit for the minor. If the claimant has valid evidence (bayyinah 'adilah), the guardian may settle for the equivalent value or slightly more (within the range customarily overlooked, taghabun), because the minor benefits by avoiding loss of the asset. If the claimant has no evidence or weak evidence, the conciliation is invalid because the guardian would be giving away the minor's property without compensation, which is harmful. If the guardian himself is the claimant for the minor (e.g., claiming that the property belongs to the minor), and he has no evidence, he may settle freely because he is obtaining a benefit without any loss. If he has evidence, he may only settle for the equivalent or less, because the minor has a strong claim. The principle is that guardianship must be exercised in the minor's best interest (nazariyya), supported by the Quranic verse on orphan's property.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): Conciliation: a contract to settle a claim, here by a guardian on behalf of a minor.
التغابن (al-taghabun): The amount that people customarily overlook in transactions due to ignorance or difficulty in exact valuation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a third party claims a house from a minor and has valid evidence, the guardian's conciliation is valid if the settlement amount is:
A) Any amount the guardian chooses.
B) Exactly half the value.
C) Equal to the value or up to the amount of taghabun.
D) Less than the value by any amount.

Key Takeaway
A guardian may settle a claim with evidence for the equivalent value or a slight customary excess to preserve the minor's asset.

Conciliation for a Minor: No Evidence or Father as Claimant
Ruling with proof: invalidity of sulh without evidence, and rules for father as claimant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why conciliation is invalid when the claimant lacks valid evidence, and distinguish the case where the father claims for the minor with or without evidence.

Core Idea
Without evidence, conciliation is harmful and invalid; a father claiming for his minor without evidence may settle freely to acquire property.

If the claimant has no evidence or their witnesses are unjust, the guardian cannot make sulh because it would be donating the minor's property. The text then contrasts the case where the father himself claims a property for the minor: without evidence, he may settle in any manner since it brings pure benefit. But if the father has evidence, he may only settle for the equivalent or less, because the minor now has a strong claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: claimant without valid evidence
The second scenario: the claimant has no evidence (bayyinah) or his evidence is not just (ghayr 'ādila). In this case, the ruling changes.
2
Understand the ruling: conciliation is invalid
The text says 'it is not valid' (lā yaṣiḥ). The reason is that the guardian would be giving away the minor's property without compensation, because the claimant is not entitled to anything.
This would be harm, not benefit.
3
Apply the Quranic proof
The author cites the verse 'Do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner' (Quran 6:152) to emphasize that guardianship must be exercised beneficially.
This verse is the primary textual proof.
4
Switch to the father as claimant for the minor
Now consider the case where the father himself claims that a property belongs to the minor (e.g., he demands it from a third party). Two subcases: with or without evidence.
5
Father without evidence: conciliation is permissible freely
If the father has no evidence, he may settle for any amount because he is acquiring property for the minor without any loss. It is pure benefit.
No harm, so no restriction.
6
Father with evidence: conciliation restricted
If the father has just evidence (bayyinah 'ādila), the minor's claim is strong (in the meaning of ownership). Therefore, the guardian may only settle for the equivalent value or less than taghabun to avoid giving away too much.
The ruling mirrors the third-party claimant with evidence scenario.

If a man claims a house and the father (of a minor) makes conciliation on behalf of the minor regarding the minor's property: if the claimant has evidence, it is permissible if it is for the equivalent value or more than what people normally overlook (mutaghābin), because there is a benefit for the minor in it, which is the safety of the asset for him; because if he did not make conciliation, the claimant would take it. So it is secure with the equivalent and with the amount customarily overlooked, because it cannot be avoided. He said: And if the claimant has no evidence or it is not just ('adila), it is not valid, because he would be donating the minor's property through the conciliation, not purchasing it, because the claimant was not entitled to anything of his property without the conciliation, so there is no benefit for the minor in this conciliation, rather there is harm, so it is not permissible because guardianship is based on consideration (nazariyya). Allah says: 'Do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner' (Quran 6:152). If the father himself is the claimant for the minor and has no evidence, it is permissible in any case, because he did not establish ownership for the minor in what he claims, nor the meaning of ownership, which is the ability to take. So he is acquiring property for him without anything leaving the minor's ownership in exchange, so it is pure benefit. If he has just evidence, it is not permissible except for the equivalent or less than what is customarily overlooked, because it has become in the meaning of ownership due to his ability to take by just evidence. Commentary
This passage details the conditions under which a guardian (father or executor) can enter into a conciliation (sulh) on behalf of a minor when a third party claims property. The key is the presence of benefit for the minor. If the claimant has valid evidence (bayyinah 'adilah), the guardian may settle for the equivalent value or slightly more (within the range customarily overlooked, taghabun), because the minor benefits by avoiding loss of the asset. If the claimant has no evidence or weak evidence, the conciliation is invalid because the guardian would be giving away the minor's property without compensation, which is harmful. البينة العادلة (al-bayyinah al-'adilah): Just evidence: testimony from upright witnesses accepted by the court.
الوصي (al-wasi): Executor: a guardian appointed by the father to manage the minor's affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Without evidence, a guardian cannot settle a claim against a minor because it is harmful; but a father claiming for a minor without evidence may settle freely to acquire property.

Imam's Authority to Cut a Public Road
Ruling on imam's power to alter public road for maslahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an imam appointed by the caliph may alter a public road and identify the basis (dalil) for this authority in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
An appointed imam holds the caliph's authority over public welfare, allowing road alterations for maslahah without harm.

This lesson examines the extent of an imam's authority over public roads. The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states a ruling and then provides a rational justification based on the principle of maslahah. We will unpack both the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Read the opening matn: the imam may cut a person from the main road (i.e., alter the road) provided it does not harm passersby.
The condition of 'no harm' is key.
2
Locate the basis (dalil)
The commentator states that the imam has authority to act for public welfare (wilayah al-ammah). This is the rational proof.
The proof is qiyas based on the imam's general authority.
3
Examine the analogy (qiyas)
The appointed imam is treated like the caliph because he is his deputy. This deputyship extends to matters of public interest.
The analogy is drawn from the caliph's role as deputy of the Prophet (saw).
4
Consider the practical application
The commentator gives an example: if the imam sees benefit in including part of the road into a mosque or vice versa, he may do so.
This illustrates the scope of the authority.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
Other schools may restrict such authority to the caliph alone, but Hanafis extend it to his appointed deputies.
Understanding the dispute highlights the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (للإمام الذي ولاه الخليفة أن يقطع انسانا من الطريق الجادة إن لم يضر ذلك بالمارة) لأن للأمام ولاية التصرف في حق الكافة فيما فيه نظر للمسلمين فإذا رأى في مصلحة لهم كان له أن يفعله من غير أن يلحق ضرراً بأحد ألا ترى أنه إذا رأى أن يدخل بعض الطريق في المسجد أو بالعكس وكان في ذلك مصلحة للمسلمين كان له أن يفعل ذلك، والإمام الذي ولاه الخليفة بمنزلة الخليفة لأنه نائبه فكان فيه مثله .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (For the imam appointed by the caliph to cut a person from the main road, provided it does not harm the passersby) because the imam has authority to act regarding the public in matters concerning the welfare of Muslims. So if he sees a benefit for them, he may do it without causing harm to anyone. Do you not see that if he sees fit to include part of the road into the mosque or vice versa, and there is benefit for Muslims in that, he may do it. And the imam appointed by the caliph is like the caliph because he is his deputy, so he is like him in this matter.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn expanded by the commentator. It addresses the authority of an imam appointed by the caliph to alter a public road, such as narrowing it or cutting off a portion, as long as it does not harm the passersby. The proof is rational (maslahah): the imam has general authority to manage public affairs for the benefit of Muslims. The commentator draws an analogy with adjusting the boundary between a mosque and a road. The appointed imam has the same authority as the caliph because he is his deputy. This ruling is based on the principle of public welfare (maslahah mursalah) and is within the discretionary authority of the ruler.

Key Terms
الإمام الذي ولاه الخليفة (al-imam alladhi wallahu al-khalifah): The imam appointed by the caliph, i.e., a governor or judge with delegated authority.
الطريق الجادة (al-tariq al-jaddah): The main public road (thoroughfare).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Only if the road is unused

Key Takeaway
An imam appointed by the caliph has authority to alter public roads for public welfare, provided no harm to passersby.

Liability for Indirect Harm from Property Use: Tasabbub vs. Ta'addi
Ruling on liability when neighbor's wall is damaged by well/cesspool

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a neighbor's property is damaged by an indirect cause (tasabbub) originating from your lawful property, and distinguish it from transgression (ta'addi) that creates liability.

Core Idea
Mere causation without transgression does not create liability when acting within one's own property.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school: if a person digs a well or cesspool on his own land and it causes a neighbor's wall to collapse, the owner is not forced to remove it and is not liable for the damage. This ruling hinges on a key legal distinction between direct causation and mere indirect causation, and on the concept of transgression (ta'addi). By the end of this lesson, you will understand this distinction and be able to apply it to similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed claim
Read the passage and identify what action the owner performed and what the neighbor demands.
The act is digging a well or cesspool on one's own property. The neighbor demands its removal and claims damages for the collapsed wall.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Express the ruling concisely: the owner is not forced to remove the well/cesspool, and he is not liable for the wall's collapse.
3
Analyze the legal basis
Identify the two reasons given in the text: (1) the owner acted in his exclusive property (tasarruf fi khalis milkihi), and (2) this is mere causation (tasabbub), which does not incur liability unless there is transgression (ta'addi).
Transgression is exemplified by placing a stone on a public road.
4
Distinguish tasabbub from ta'addi
Understand the difference: tasabbub is an indirect cause without wrongful act; ta'addi is acting beyond one's rights or in a prohibited manner. The owner's act in his own property is not transgression.
5
Apply to similar scenarios
Consider other cases where an action on one's property indirectly harms a neighbor, and test whether transgression exists.
For example, building a wall that blocks light – may be treated differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : اتخذ بئرا في ملكه أو بالوعة فنز منها حائط جاره فطلب تحويله لا يجبر عليه وإن سقط الحائط منه لم يضمن) لأنه تصرف في خالص ملكه ولأن هذا تسبب وبه لا يجب الضمان إلا إذا كان متعدياً كوضع الحجر على الطريق واتخاذ ذلك ملكه ليس بتعد في فلا يضمن .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If a person digs a well in his property or a cesspool, and the wall of his neighbor collapses because of it, and he demands its removal, he is not forced to do so. And if the wall collapses because of it, he is not liable) because he acted in his own exclusive property, and this is a mere cause (tasabbub), and liability is not obligatory for mere causation unless there is transgression (ta'addi), like placing a stone on the road. Doing so in his own property is not transgression, so he is not liable.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn expanded by the commentator. It deals with a situation where someone digs a well or a cesspool (soakaway) on his own land, and this causes the neighbor's wall to collapse (due to moisture or subsidence). The neighbor demands that the well/cesspool be removed or relocated. The owner is not compelled to do so, and if the wall collapses, he is not liable for damages. The reason is that he acted within his own property rights, and mere causation (tasabbub) without transgression (ta'addi) does not create liability. An example of transgression would be placing a stone on a public road (which is not one's property). Since the owner acted in his own property, there is no transgression.

Key Terms
تسبب (tasabbub): Causation (indirect cause) that does not directly cause harm; mere causation without direct action does not entail liability in Hanafi law unless there is transgression.
تعد (ta'addi): Transgression or exceeding proper bounds; acting inappropriately or unlawfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man digs a well on his property, which causes his neighbor's wall to crack. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He must remove the well and pay for the damage
B) He is not forced to remove it and is not liable
C) He must pay for the damage but can keep the well
D) He is liable only if the neighbor warned him

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, acting within one's own property without transgression does not incur liability for indirect harm.

No Liability for Taking a Debtor from Another's Hand
Ruling on no liability for taking a debtor from a captor, expanded with analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the snatcher of a debtor is not liable, explain the principle of 'fi'l al-mukhtar' (act of a free agent), and apply the same principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Liability only attaches to direct causation; the act of a free agent breaks the causal chain.

You might think that anyone who helps a debtor escape from his creditor should be liable for the debt. But Hanafi fiqh says otherwise. This lesson unpacks an important principle: the act of a free agent (mukhtar) severs the chain of causation. We will examine the ruling and its reasoning through several analogies, sharpening your ability to distinguish direct from indirect causation in tort law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: if a person seizes his debtor and another person snatches the debtor from his hand, the snatcher is not liable. Confirm that the ruling is 'no liability' (adam al-daman).
The ruling applies even if the debtor escapes and is lost.
2
Understand the reasoning (fi'l al-mukhtar)
The loss (the debtor's escape) is not caused by the snatcher's act; the debtor is a free agent who chooses to run away. The snatcher merely enables, but does not compel, the escape. In Hanafi tort law, the act of a mukhtar breaks the causal link.
This principle is distinct from direct coercion (al-ikrah al-mulji').
3
Examine the analogies (qiyas)
The commentary compares three cases: (1) untying a slave who then runs away, (2) pointing out a thief, (3) holding a person who is then killed by an enemy. In each, the intermediate act is by a free agent, so the original actor is not liable.
These analogies are all based on the shared illa: the final harmful act is by a mukhtar.
4
Distinguish from liability-attracting scenarios
Contrast with cases where the actor directly causes the loss, e.g., physically pushing the debtor into escape, or using force that overwhelms the debtor's free will. There, liability may attach. The key is direct causation versus mere enabling.
This distinction is crucial for applying the principle correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو أخذ غريمة فنزعه انسان من يده لم يضمن) أي لا يضمن النازع فلا يضاف إليه التلف كما إذا حل قيد العبد فأبق فإن الحال لا يضمن لأن التلف لم يحصل بفعله وإنما حصل بفعل العبد وهو مختار، وكذا إذا دل السارق فإن الفعل حصل بفعل السرقة لا بدلالته وكمن أمسك هارباً من عدو حتى قتله العدو فإن الممسك لا يجب عليه الضمان فكذا هذا .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And if one seizes a debtor, then another person snatches him from his hand, he is not liable) meaning the snatcher is not liable, so the loss (of the debtor) is not attributed to him. Just as if someone unties a slave's shackle and the slave runs away, the untier is not liable because the loss did not occur by his act but by the act of the slave, who is free (mukhtar). Similarly, if one points out a thief (to someone), the actual theft is by the thief's act, not by the pointing. And like one who holds a person fleeing from an enemy until the enemy kills him; the holder is not liable. So too this case.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn expanded by the commentator. It deals with a scenario where a person (A) has taken hold of his debtor (the person who owes him money) in order to claim the debt. Then another person (B) snatches that debtor from A's hand (freeing him). The question is whether B is liable if the debtor escapes or is lost. The ruling is that B is not liable because the loss is not directly caused by B's act; rather, the debtor is a free agent who chooses to escape. The commentator draws analogies: (1) If someone unties a slave's shackle and the slave runs away, the untier is not liable because the slave's escape is his own act. (2) If someone points out a thief to a victim, the thief's act of stealing is not attributed to the pointer. (3) If someone holds back a person fleeing from an enemy until the enemy kills him, the holder is not liable for the killing because the enemy's act is independent. In all cases, the direct cause is the act of a free agent, not the indirect cause.

Key Terms
غريم (gharim): Debtor; a person who owes a debt.
فعل المختار (fi'l al-mukhtar): Act of a free, responsible agent. In Hanafi tort law, liability does not attach to one who only enables the act of a free agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi ruling for a person who snatches a debtor from his creditor's hand, allowing the debtor to escape?
A) The snatcher is liable for the debt because he freed the debtor.
B) The snatcher is not liable because the debtor's escape is his own free act.
C) The snatcher is liable only if he knew about the debt.
D) The snatcher is liable but the creditor must prove intent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a person who enables the escape of a free debtor through indirect means is not liable for the debt because the debtor's own free act breaks the causal chain.

Forced Payment Under Sultan's Threat
Ruling on liability under duress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on liability when property is transferred under duress from a sultan, and identify who bears liability based on the recipient's voluntariness.

Core Idea
Fear of severed hand makes payment void of liability — coercion transfers responsibility to the coercer or willing taker.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with a harsh scenario: a ruler forces you to hand over someone else's property under threat of physical mutilation. Are you liable? Hanafi law says no — the real liability falls on either the coercer or the recipient, depending on the recipient's choice. Let's break down the ruling and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the threat
Who holds the property? Who threatens? What is the threat? (cutting hand, 50 lashes). The threat must be severe and imminent to constitute duress.
The threat of bodily harm (hand amputation or flogging) qualifies as duress in Hanafi law.
2
Apply the rule of coercion (ikrah)
Hanafis rule that the coerced payer's will is nullified; he is not liable for the loss. Liability shifts to the coercer.
Coercion negates consent; the coercer is the true wrongdoer.
3
Classify the recipient's state
Was the recipient also coerced or did he accept voluntarily? This determines where the owner can seek compensation.
If the recipient is a free agent (mukhtar), the owner may sue him; if not, only the sultan.
4
State the final ruling
Payer not liable. Owner may choose: coercer or willing recipient (if mukhtar). If recipient coerced, only coercer liable.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (في يده مال انسان فقال له سلطان ادفع إلى هذا المال وإلا اقطع يدك أو أضربك خمسين فدفع لم يضمن) أي لا يضمن الدافع لأنه مكره عليه فكان الضمان على المكره أو على الآخذ أيهما شاء المالك إذا كان الآخذ مختاراً وإلا فعلى المكره فقط .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If in his hand is the property of someone, and the sultan tells him: 'Pay this money to him, otherwise I will cut off your hand or strike you fifty times,' and he pays, he is not liable) meaning the payer is not liable because he is coerced. Liability is upon the coercer (sultan) or the taker (recipient), whichever the owner wishes, if the taker was a free agent (mukhtar). Otherwise (if the taker was also coerced), liability is only upon the coercer.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn expanded by the commentator. It discusses a scenario where a person (A) is holding someone else's property (as an amana, e.g., as a deposit). The sultan threatens A with severe harm (cutting off hand or 50 lashes) unless A pays that property to a third party (B). A complies and pays. The ruling is that A is not liable for the property because he was coerced (mukrah). The liability (daman) is upon either the coercer (the sultan) or the recipient (B), at the option of the owner. If B was also coerced (i.e., forced to accept), then liability is only on the coercer. This is based on Hanafi principles of coercion: the coerced person is excused, and the actual wrongdoer (coercer or voluntary recipient) bears responsibility.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): A person under duress (coercion). In Hanafi law, the coerced is not liable for property he is forced to transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person is forced by the sultan to pay another's property under threat of losing his hand, and he pays, who is liable?
A) The payer is liable.
B) Only the sultan is liable.
C) The owner may choose between the sultan and the recipient if the recipient accepted willingly.
D) Both the payer and the sultan are jointly liable.

Key Takeaway
A person forced by a ruler to pay another's property under threat of severe harm is not liable; the owner may claim from the coercer or the willing recipient.

Ruling on Permissibility of Racing (Musābaqah)
Ruling statement on permissible competitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on racing with horses, camels, on foot, and archery, and identify the condition that makes such contests permissible.

Core Idea
Racing for training and jihad is permissible, provided it is free from gambling.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā’iq states the basic ruling that four types of racing are permissible: horses, camels, foot races, and archery. While competition is generally allowed, the Hanafi school places conditions to ensure it remains lawful. In this lesson, we will explore the ruling and its underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is competitive racing (musābaqah) and the ruling is permissible (jā’iz).
Jā’iz here implies mubah, not wajib.
2
Recognize the types mentioned
The text lists four categories: horses, camels, foot races, and archery.
These are explicitly mentioned by the Prophet (peace be upon him) as encouraged contests.
3
Understand the basis for permissibility
Permissibility is rooted in the sunnah and the need to train for jihad and personal skill development.
Hadith: 'There is no competition except in archery, camel racing, and horse racing.'
4
Identify the condition for permissibility
The contest must be free from gambling (qimār). The prize, if any, must come from a third party or one participant's own wealth without mutual wagering.
Gambling would make the contest haram.
5
Contrast with prohibited contests
Any contest involving gambling or harm is impermissible. The ruling is conditional on absence of these factors.
The Hanafi school emphasizes the intent behind the contest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسابقة بالفرس والإبل والأرجل والرمي جائزة
And racing with horses, camels, feet, and archery is permissible.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This short passage is a ruling statement that competitive racing involving horses, camels, on foot, and archery are permissible (ja'iz) in the Hanafi school. This is based on the general permissibility of such contests that are beneficial for training and jihad.

Key Terms
مسابقة (musābaqah): Competitive racing or contest involving a prize.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Horses
B) Camels
C) Donkeys
D) Archery

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits competitive racing in horses, camels, foot races, and archery, provided it is free from gambling and serves beneficial training.

Rulings on Racing and Betting: Permissibility and Prohibition of Gambling
Clarifying permissibility of racing and conditions for permissible betting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on racing with prizes, distinguish between permissible one-sided betting and prohibited two-sided betting (qimar), and apply the condition of the muhallil.

Core Idea
Two-sided betting is gambling (qimar) and haram; one-sided betting is permissible by istihsan even though qiyas forbids it.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Islamic rules of competition and betting. Racing on horses, camels, on foot, and archery is permissible. The key legal question is when a prize (jual) can be offered. The text distinguishes between two-sided betting, which is gambling and haram, and one-sided betting, which is allowed by istihsan. It also introduces a unique Hanafi concept: the muhallil, a third competitor who can make a two-sided arrangement permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify permissible forms of racing
The text explicitly permits racing with horses, camels, feet, and archery. This is the baseline.
Racing without any prize is permissible in all things.
2
Distinguish between one-sided and two-sided prize conditions
Two-sided betting (both parties put a prize) is qimar (gambling) and haram. One-sided betting (only one side offers a prize) is permissible by istihsan.
The key difference is mutual risk vs. one-sided promise.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith 'لا سبق إلا في خف أو نعل أو حافر' restricts permissible prize to these categories. Also, the Prophet's permission to Salama to race a man supports racing.
The hadith does not specify one-sided or two-sided; the Hanafis use istihsan to refine.
4
Understand the role of istihsan
Qiyas would prohibit one-sided betting due to risk (ta'liq al-milk bi al-khatar). Istihsan overrides because the risk is one-sided and the activity serves the need of exercise for war.
This is a classic Hanafi method: preferring istihsan over qiyas in muamalat.
5
Analyze the muhallil concept
A third competitor (muhallil) can make a two-sided arrangement permissible if he has a genuine chance to win. If he wins, he takes both prizes; if he loses, he pays nothing.
This removes the mutual gambling element because the muhallil stakes nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والمسابقة بالفرس والإبل والأرجل والرمي جائزة وحرم شرط الجعل من الجانبين لا من أحد الجانبين) لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «لا سبق إلا في خف أو نعل أو حافر (۱) وأذن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بن الأكوع أن يسابق رجلاً كان لا يسابق أبداً فسبقه سلمة بن الأكوع. وقال الزهري : كانت المسابقة بين أصحاب رسول الله الله بالخيل والركاب والأرجل. ولأن الغزاة يحتاجون إلى رياضة خيلهم وأنفسهم والتعلم للكلب والقدد مباح . قال رحمه الله : (وحرم شرط الجعل من الجانبين لا من أحد الجانبين) لما روى ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما أن النبي الله سبق بالخيل وراهن ومعنى شرط الجعل من الجانبين أن يقول إن سبق فرسك فلك علي كذا وإن سبق فرسي فلي عليك كذا وهو قمار فلا يجوز لأن القمار من القمر الذي يزاد تارة وينقص أخرى وسمي القمار قماراً لأن كل واحد من القمارين ممن يجوز أن يذهب ماله إلى صاحبه ويجوز أن يستفيد مال صاحبه فيجوز الازدياد والنقصان في كل واحد منهما فصار ذلك قماراً وهو حرام بالنص ولا كذلك إذا شرط من جانب واحد بأن يقول إن كذا وإن سبقتك فلا شيء لي عليك لأن النقصان والزيادة لا يمكن فيهما وإنما أحدهما يمكن الزيادة وفي الأخرى النقصان فلا يكون مقامرة لأن المقام مفاعلة منه فيقتضي أن يكون من الجانبين، وإذا لم يكن في معناه جاز استحساناً لما روينا . والقياس أنه لا يجوز لما فيه من تعليق الملك على الخطر ولهذا لا تجوز فيما عدا الأربعة المذكورة في الكتاب كالبغل وإن كان الجعل مشروطاً من أحد الجانبين. وفي الحديث إشارة إليه لأنه خصص هؤلاء والمراد به الاستباق بلا جعل يجوز في كل شيء ولا يمكن إلحاق ما شرط فيه الجعل لأنه ليس في معناه لأن المانع فيه من وجهين القمار والتعليق بالخطر. وفي الآخر من وجه واحد هو التعليق بالخطر لا غير فليس بمثل له حتى يقاس عليه . وشرطه أن تكون الغاية مما تتحملها الفرس، وكذا شرطه أن يكون في كل واحد من الفرسين احتمال السبق، وأما إذا علم أن أحدهما يسبق لا محالة فلا يجوز لأنه إنما جاز لحاجة الرياضة على خلاف القياس وليس في هذا ايجاب المال للغير على نفسه بشرط لا منفعة فيه فلا يجوز، ولو شرط الجعل من الجانبين وأدخلا ثالثاً محللاً جاز إذا كان فرس المحلل كفؤاً لفرسيهما يجوز أن يسبق أو سبق فلا محالة وإلا فلا يجوز لقوله من أدخل فرساً بين الفرسين وهو لا يأمن يسبق فلا بأس»(۲) رواه أحمد وأبو داود وغيرهما. وصورة إدخال المحلل أن يقول للثالث إن سبقتنا فالمالان لك، وإن سبقناك فلا شيء لنا عليك ولكن الشرط الذي شرطناه بينهما وهو أن أيهما سبق كان له الجعل على صاحبه باقٍ
He (the author) said: 'And racing with horses, camels, feet, and archery is permissible. And the condition of the prize from both sides is prohibited, not from one side.' Because of his statement صلى الله عليه وسلم: 'There is no prize except in hoof, sandal, or arrow.' And the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم gave permission to Salama ibn al-Akwa' to race a man who never raced, and Salama ibn al-Akwa' beat him. Al-Zuhri said: Racing among the Companions of the Messenger of Allah was with horses, camels, and feet. And because the warriors need to exercise their horses and themselves, and learning for dogs and arrows is permissible. He (the author) said: 'And the condition of the prize from both sides is prohibited, not from one side.' Because it is related from Ibn Umar that the Prophet raced horses and wagered. The meaning of stipulating the prize from both sides is that one says: 'If your horse wins, you have such-and-such from me, and if my horse wins, I have such-and-such from you.' This is gambling (qimar) and is not permissible. Because gambling comes from al-qamar which increases sometimes and decreases other times, and it is called qimar because each of the two gamblers is such that his money may go to his companion and he may gain his companion's money; so increase and decrease are possible for each one, so it becomes gambling, and it is prohibited by text. It is not so when the condition is from one side, such as saying: 'If you win, you have such-and-such from me; but if I win, nothing for me from you.' Because decrease and increase are not possible for both; rather, for one increase is possible and for the other decrease, so it is not gambling because 'muqamara' is a mutual action (mufa'ala) that requires it to be from both sides. And when it is not in its meaning, it is permissible by istihsan, based on what we narrated. The analogy (qiyas) is that it is not permissible because it involves suspending ownership on a risk, and for this reason it is not permissible in other than the four mentioned in the book, such as mules, even if the prize is conditioned from one side. In the hadith there is an indication to this because it specified these [animals/weapons], and what is meant is racing without a prize is permissible in everything, and what involves a prize cannot be annexed to it because it is not in its meaning; the impediment in this case is from two aspects: gambling and suspension on risk. In the other [one-sided] it is from one aspect, which is suspension on risk only, so it is not equivalent to be analogized. Its condition is that the distance should be such that the horse can bear, and also that each of the two horses has the potential to win. As for when it is known that one will definitely win, it is not permissible, because it is only allowed for the need of exercise contrary to analogy, and in this there is obligation of money to others on oneself with a condition of no benefit, so it is not permissible. If they stipulated the prize from both sides and brought in a third as a 'muhallil' (legalizer), it is permissible if the muhallil's horse is competitive with theirs, so that it may win or be overtaken definitely; otherwise it is not permissible, because of his statement: 'Whoever enters a horse between two horses and he is not safe from being overtaken, there is no harm.' Reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and others. The form of entering the muhallil is that he says to the third: 'If you win against us, both prizes are for you; but if we win against you, nothing is owed to us by you.' But the condition that they stipulated between them, that whichever wins gets the prize from the other, remains.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rules of racing and betting. The matn states that racing with horses, camels, by foot, and archery is permissible. However, stipulating a prize (jual) from both sides is prohibited (haram) because it constitutes gambling (qimar). The proof is the hadith: 'لا سبق إلا في خف أو نعل أو حافر' meaning no prize is permissible except in these categories (camel, foot, horse). The text then elaborates: when only one side stipulates a prize, it is permissible by istihsan (juristic preference), though qiyas would forbid it due to risk. The key distinction is that two-sided bets involve mutual risk of loss and gain (gambling), whereas a one-sided bet is a promise of reward for a specific outcome, which is not gambling. The text also discusses conditions: the distance must be manageable, and both horses must have a chance of winning. If one is certain to win, it is not allowed because it lacks the purpose of exercise and is a means of taking money without benefit. Finally, the text introduces the concept of a 'muhallil' (a third party) who enters the race to make a two-sided bet permissible. The muhallil stakes nothing from his side; if he wins, he gets both prizes; if he loses, he pays nothing. This transforms the gambling into a lawful competition.

Key Terms
السبق (al-sabaq): The prize or stake in a competition, especially racing.
الجعل (al-ju'lu): The prize or reward stipulated for winning a race.
القمار (al-qimar): Gambling, defined as a transaction where each party risks losing or gaining money.
المحلل (al-muhallil): A third party introduced to a two-sided bet to make it permissible, by staking nothing but winning both prizes if he wins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what condition makes a two-sided betting on a horse race permissible?
A) The prize must be from both sides equally.
B) A third horse (muhallil) must be entered with a chance to win.
C) The distance must be measured.
D) The horses must be of equal speed.

Key Takeaway
Two-sided betting is qimar and haram; one-sided betting is permissible by istihsan; a muhallil can make two-sided betting permissible under conditions.

Rulings on Wearing Black Clothing and Turbans
Recommended dress practices with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wearing black clothing and turbans, and identify the proofs from the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Wearing black and letting the turban tail hang is recommended; fine clothing is a blessing, not forbidden.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on men's dress, particularly the recommendation to wear black and the proper manner of wearing a turban. We will examine the evidence from the Quran and the practice of the Salaf, as well as the important principle of avoiding imitation of disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rulings and their hukm levels
Read the passage and list the various rulings mentioned: wearing black, turban tail length, wearing caps, and wearing good clothes. Determine the hukm level for each (sunnah, mubah, etc.).
The text explicitly says 'يسن لبس السواد' (it is sunnah to wear black) and 'ويستحب للرجل أن يلبس أحسن ثيابه' (it is mustahab for a man to wear his best clothes).
2
Locate the proofs
Find the evidence cited for each ruling. For black clothing, the proof is a hadith from al-Siyar al-Kabir. For wearing good clothes, the proof is the Quranic verse (al-A'raf:32) and a hadith.
The passage also quotes the hadith 'Whoever imitates a people is one of them' to warn against tashabbuh.
3
Understand the method of wearing the turban
Focus on the section about the turban: how to fold it and the length of the tail. Note the different opinions and the chosen view (to mid-back).
The story of Imam Muhammad removing his black turban is a personal preference, not a change in ruling.
4
Note the exception regarding imitation of disbelievers
Examine the example from al-Jami' al-Saghir about buying something on Nawruz. Understand that intention matters: if one intends to venerate a non-Islamic festival, it can be kufr; otherwise, it is not.
This shows the principle of tashabbuh is contextual and intention-based.
5
Appreciate the recommendation to wear good clothes as gratitude
The passage concludes with the encouragement to wear fine clothes as a manifestation of Allah's blessing. Note the practice of Abu Hanifa and the Prophet ﷺ.
This counters the misconception that Islam discourages good dress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العمامة بين الكتفين إلى وسط الظهر للشاب العالم أن يتقدم على الشيخ الجاهل ولحافظ القرآن أن يختم في كل أربعين يوما . قال صلى الله عليه وسلم (من تشبه بقوم فهو منهم (۱) وقال في الجامع الأصغر : رجل اشترى يوم النيروز شيئاً يشتريه الكفرة منه وهو لم يكن يشتريه قبل ذلك إن أراد به تعظيم ذلك اليوم كما تعظمه المشركون كفر ، وإن أراد الأكل والشرب والتنعم لا يكفر . وقد صح قال رحمه الله : ولا بأس بلبس القلانس لما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان له قلانس يلبسها ذلك ذكره في الذخيرة. قال رحمه الله : (ويسن) لبس السواد وارسال ذنب العمامة بين الكتفين إلى وسط الظهر لأن محمداً رحمه الله ذكر في السير الكبير في باب الغنائم حديثاً يدل على أن لبس السواد مستحب، ومن أراد أن يجدد اللف للعمامة ينبغي له أن ينقضها كوراً فكوراً فإن ذلك أحسن من رفعها على الرأس والقائها في الأرض دفعة واحدة، وأن المستحب إرسال ذنب العمامة بين الكتفين واختلفوا في مقدار الذنب قيل شبر، وقيل إلى وسط الظهر، وقيل إلى موضع الجلوس. وكان محمد رحمه الله يتعمم بالعمامة السوداء فدخلت عليه يوماً مستورة فبقيت تنظر إلى وجهه وهي متحيرة فقال لها : ما شأنك؟ فقالت : أتعجب من بياض وجهك تحت سواد عمامتك فوضعها عن رأسه ولم يتعمم بالعمامة السوداء بعد ذلك، ويستحب للرجل أن يلبس أحسن ثيابه، وكان أبو حنيفة يأمر أصحابه بذلك ويلبس بأربعمائة دينار وأباح الله تعالى الزينة بقوله قل من حرم زينة الله التي أخرج لعباده [الاعراف : ٣٢] وقال له إن الله تعالى إذا أنعم على عبد أحب أن يرى أثر نعمته عليه»(۲) وقد خرج رسول الله وعليه رداء قيمته أربعة آلاف درهم وربما قام إلى الصلاة وعليه رداء قيمته أربعة آلاف درهم. (۱) رواه أبو داود في كتاب اللباس باب ٤. أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٥٠). (۲) رواه الترمذي في كتاب الأدب باب ٥٤. أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٣١١، ٤٣٨).
The turban (trailing) between the shoulders to the middle of the back. For a young learned man to step forward over an ignorant elder, and for a memorizer of the Quran to complete (the recitation) every forty days. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever imitates a people is one of them.' And it is said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man buys something on Nawruz that the disbelievers buy from it, and he never bought it before; if he intends thereby to venerate that day as the polytheists venerate it, he becomes an infidel; if he intends eating, drinking, and enjoyment, he does not become an infidel. And it is authenticated. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: There is no harm in wearing caps because it is narrated that the Prophet ﷺ had caps that he wore; that is mentioned in al-Dhakhira. He said: It is sunnah to wear black and to let the tail of the turban hang between the shoulders to the middle of the back, because Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in al-Siyar al-Kabir in the chapter on spoils a hadith indicating that wearing black is mustahab. And whoever wants to make a fresh wrap of the turban should undo it fold by fold, for that is better than lifting it onto the head and throwing it to the ground all at once. And it is mustahab to let the tail of the turban hang between the shoulders, and they differed about the length: some said a handspan, some said to the middle of the back, some said to the sitting place. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) used to wear a black turban, and one day a female cloth entered upon him and kept looking at his face in confusion. He asked, 'What is the matter?' She said, 'I am amazed at the whiteness of your face under the blackness of your turban.' So he removed it from his head and did not wear a black turban after that. And it is mustahab for a man to wear his best clothes, and Abu Hanifa used to command his companions to do that and wore clothes worth four hundred dinars. And Allah the Exalted permitted adornment with His saying: 'Say, who has forbidden the adornment of Allah which He has brought forth for His servants' [al-A'raf:32]. And he said: Indeed Allah, when He bestows a blessing on a servant, loves to see the effect of His blessing upon him. And the Messenger of Allah came out wearing a cloak worth four thousand dirhams, and sometimes he stood for prayer wearing a cloak worth four thousand dirhams. (1) Narrated by Abu Dawud in the Book of Dress, chapter 4. Ahmad in his Musnad (2/50). (2) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Etiquette, chapter 54. Ahmad in his Musnad (2/311, 438).
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the etiquette of dress and related matters. It begins by stating three rulings in summary. The author then elaborates, first on the turban and black clothing. He quotes the hadith 'Whoever imitates a people is one of them' to warn against imitating disbelievers, especially in their festivals. Then he says there is no harm in wearing caps because the Prophet ﷺ wore them. He then states that wearing black is sunnah, citing a hadith from Muhammad al-Shaybani's al-Siyar al-Kabir. The method of folding the turban is explained: it should be undone fold by fold rather than thrown. The length of the trailing end is disputed; the choice here is to the middle of the back. There is a story of Imam Muhammad initially wearing a black turban but stopping when a woman commented on the contrast. Then the author says it is mustahab for a man to wear his best clothes, citing Abu Hanifa's practice and the Quranic verse about adornment, and a hadith that Allah loves to see the effect of His blessings. The Prophet ﷺ himself wore expensive garments. The passage thus establishes the recommended practices for a Muslim man's dress.

Key Terms
العمامة (al-‘imāmah): A turban, a headwrap worn by men, specifically its trailing tail (dhunab) is discussed.
السواد (al-sawād): Black color, referring to black clothing.
تشبه (tashabbuh): Imitating or resembling others, especially disbelievers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on wearing black clothing?
A) It is fard
B) It is haram
C) It is sunnah
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
Wearing black and fine clothes is recommended in Hanafi fiqh, as long as it is not out of imitation of disbelievers.
